RATING: 4
Update: Rating 6->4

After picking up Call to Arms, what I had anticipated would make the game more satisfying to me, I am now over the Battlelore hype. CtA should've been included in the base game, and even then, it is still a very weak scenario generator. 

Upon seeing the new goblin and dwarf units (which cost $40 altogether, ouch!) I see that none of the expansions are going to add anything besides some pretty minor tweaks. Small changes in the boldness rules, minor alterations to attacks, etc. In other words, nothing new.

After playing some heavier wargames, I've come to realize just how small the decision space is for this game. After only a handful of plays, the tactics become trivial, and the lore cards don't hold any tension.

Very, very boring.

=========================

I am still really on the fence about this one. The lore cards don't excite me too much, and the three wings of the battlefield have always bothered me a bit. I think the lore cards could be made more interesting and balanced in such a way that they provide much more tension. As it stands they just feel like total chaos, particularly the Cleric cards. I do really enjoy the Theif cards.

I do enjoy the different unit types and the emphasis on formation with the boldness rules.

I still haven't tried the landmarks or Call to Arms, but I'm hoping those additions will bring the game to the level I want it to be at.

RATING: 8
2010/12
Very good, very good. Completely different to Memoir'44 despite being related. However, a couple of things kept bugging me:

1) All figures were grey in color, hence it was difficult to recognize them in a quick glance. Paintjob needed.

2) If the scenario has a equal balance, the player taking the initiative in attacking is always considered to be the underdog. Why's this? Because, you always have to keep your troops in formation or they're sent to peril and once you start moving keeping the lines takes considerable effort.

Update 2010/12/21:
3) Third thing buggin me is the inbalance between dwarves and goblins. Dwarves are just much more useful because they can operate alone. Goblins are just plain useless unless you're trying to hunt down a single figure on the board.

RATING: 3
Well, it's just not all that....It tries to be so many different kinds of things that it seems pretty good, but it is pointless compared to Commands & Colors: Ancients.  It's okay, but the games rarely have any real tension, and there's really not much to the tactics.  Lack of leaders (as in Ancients) really hurts, and I doubt bolting Heroes onto it will matter much -- might as well skip this toy and go straight to Ancients.  

I've tried Epic a few times, and I do have to say it was more fun that way.  I just think that BattleLore and Memoir both suffer from feeling like there's too little room to maneuver.

Personally, I find Battleground Fantasy Warfare to be far better.  But now that FFG owns BattleLore, let's see if they can improve it....

RATING: 8
[i]BattleLore[/i] is the perfect fantasy battles game. Limited actions, a world of possibilities -- including creating your own units by using D&D miniatures -- and some utterly creative new rules make this one a great game.

If [i]BattleLore[/i] has a failure is the time it takes to set up (and take down). It may not be [i]Puerto Rico,[/i] but it is [b]everything[/b] it needs to be, if not more.

[i]Location: Basement (FR)[/i]

RATING: 7
In the Command and Colors series this is a worthy addition but not the  best of the 4 games currently released. Battle Cry had some problems and the system doesn't seem to work as well with modern warfare (Memoir '44). Ancients certainly is more streamlined while still employing some of the interesting formation elements and tactics. The creatures in Battlelore are cool and the war council/lore cards are a nice addition. But I think the 'cleaner' Ancients is maybe a better system. Also, it seems the lore cards in Battlelore inject more chaos into a game that already has a lot of luck. Still, Battlelore is an enjoyable game.

RATING: 6.2
2011-02-15
A fun game where you have a hand of cards for orders you can issue across the sections of the battlefield, if you end up with excess right orders, chances are your opponent has excess left orders, which correspond to the same sections of the battlefield when you sit opposite of each other.  The main complaint with this game besides a huge luck element (which can be fun at times) is the incredibly long setup time (which is never fun).

RATING: 9
New release in the Command & Colors series. Interestingly, it plays VERY different from Memoir'44, one of my Top Ten games. Interesting gameplay, and the additions of Lore and the War Council are very well implemented (besides the problem with the level 3 UberPriest). It seems more luck based than it really is, because it takes some games to learn how to put battle formations to good use. Magic is important and affects your strategy but it's balanced and MOSTLY not game breaking. The Cleric is overly powerfull, though, to the point where several Cleric Spells need house rules, imho (although there seems to be no consensus on that matter). The Goblin expansions and the Hundred Years War are really cool as they allow you to deploy a completely Goblin Army and add several interesting units.

The design decisions about army customization in the Call to Arms expansion have been VERY disappointing.

(Rating downgraded from 10 to 8 then to 9)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Probably the best of the Borg systems, which actually lend themselves more to melee and magic than "shooter" types of games. BattleLore needs more play to get a firmer rating and there are some ambigiuties about Morale and Retreats, but most of those issues will be decided by the impeccbale designer and Days of Wonder. My only concerns about its replayability are the over-reliance on using Days of Wonder's website for generating scenarios, meaning that 2 people cannot meet at a game store and independently sit down and ramp up a game using pre-formed armies. This is a game that will require scheduling and planning a session, closer to an RPG than a light wargame. As well, the rules are not going to be available in PDF, so rule problems will also require add-ons, not on-the-fly published revisions. With a game this complex and add-ons coming aplenty, a more versatile rules publication system would be in order.

RATING: 7
This is a game that is neither a board or miniatures games, but a hybrid of the two.  The miniatures part is obvious while the board game part comes in the form of the straight forward rules.  There is nothing revolutionary in the game design, especially if you have played Battle Cry or Memoir '44, but the game does present some unique variations on previous editions of the rules.  The beauty of the game lies in the simple game play and its expandability.  The game opts for playability over simulation.  While dependent on luck of the dice, play does offer and require sound tactical considerations.  If you are a fan of visual games or games with bits, then this is a good candidate to consider. RATING: 8
I want to like this more, with C&C:Ancients being one of my few 10's, but I feel Ancients is the better game.  There is more of a sense of strategy with the Leaders in Ancients.  I also like the blocks better than the figures.  They're so much easier to move around.  A few times I've had my opponent's pieces end up straddling the line between my hex and his, and it takes a moment to figure out who's is who's because they are all the same.  Looking at the board is like looking at a sea of grey plastic.  The dice are annoying as well because it is difficult to tell the difference between green and blue hits at a glance, and I'm not color blind.  I don't want to seem too negative about the game as it is a good game and would probably rate higher if I didn't have Ancients. The magic system is nice and I suppose I could use the Ancients dice to see the results better, but I feel I have a better game already, so why would I want to play this?  Hopefully they will add leaders in an expansion.  I hear they are working on a "Heros of BattleLore" expansion which I hope accomplishes this.  That could raise the score a bit and bring the game off my shelf again.

RATING: 9
First, I will point out that this only didn't get 10/10 because I think the 'Call to Arms' expansion should have been included in the main game (this expansions allows players to set up 'custom' armies instead of playing set adventures).

It's just fantastic! It is FUN. The designers got it Right. It is not overly complicated, simple, contrived, realistic, brain-bending, luck-based, strategic, long, short, deep, whatever. It's just a fine example of a game that 'works', in every way you would want from a game.

Technically speaking, the bits are are great quality and there's masses of them, the mechanics are fluid and clever, luck plays an intriguing but not overbearing role and nothing is really ever complicated but somehow it feels like a 'proper' wargame with terrain effects/ troop stats/ creature types etc all getting a shout.

The replay value is immense, with scenery setup, army selection, War Council selection, special terrain, new releases etc all giving you more choices than you can cope with in each setup.

RATING: 7
Ez után a játék után a Memoir'44 nagyon leértékelődött a szememben. Szinte minden szempontból jobb a Battlelore, talán egyedül a téma az, ami a Memoirban jobban megfog. De egyébként teljesen más szintet képvisel a kettő. A Battlelore szabályának kb. negyede tartalmazza a Memoir'44 szinte teljes szabályát (minimális módosításokkal), A szabály többi része pedig rengeteget újdonságot hozzáad ehhez, teljesen más jelleget adva a Memoir-os alaprendszernek. Sokkal jobb lett a kockadobások mechanizmusa a 3 féle szín miatt. Ennek köszönhetően már a Battlelore kezdő küldetései (középkori kalandok) is jobbak, mint a Memoir. A haladó (fantasy) küldetésekbe pedig olyan új elemek is beépültek, amik teljesen más szintre emelik a játékot. A zsoldosok még kb. megfeleltethetőek a Memoir veteránjainak, de a szörnyek abban értelemszerűen nem ismertek:) (Ami viszont nem tetszik, az a pofátlanul tudatosan felépített lenyúlás, számtalan az alapszabályban előre bejelentett kieggel: az alapjáték pl. csak 1 szörnyet tartalmaz, a többit lehet gyüjtögetni, néhánynak már a képe is benne van a szabályban előre, hajrá:)
A játék igazi újítása a tudás megjelenése, ami egy teljesen új paklit hoz a játékba. A haditanács szintjeinek szétosztása nagyon sok taktikai lehetőséget rejt magában. Maga a tudás pakli pedig kimeríthetetlennek tűnő változatosságot ad a játéknak, és épp a Memoir talán legnagyobb hibáját orvosolja: ha esetleg nincs megfelelő parancskártyánk, akkor is lehet valami hasznos a tarsolyunkban, és nem ragad be a seregünk hosszú körökre.
A fentieken túl a morál is plusz színt hoz a játékba.
A 2 parti alapján kiegyenlítettnek tűnt a játék, 1 körön belül teljesítette volna a vesztes is a győzelmi célt.
Erősen ajánlott a megvétele, ha valakit nem zavar, hogy nyelvfüggő.

RATING: 7
The C&C system is probably the best out there for light, hex-n-"counter" wargaming.  BattleLore is the best looking, and eventually, will likely be the best developed of the games in this series released to date.

Out of the box, however, I believe it pales in comparison to C&C:Ancients.  C&C:A comes with a wider variety of full-featured scenarios, and the evade and battle back rules add tactical richness somewhat lacking in BattleLore.  Hence, BL gets an 8, where I rate C&C:A a 9.  I just feel that, without taking expansions into account, C&C:A is simply a better game than BL.

I have some heartburn with this game, although it has nothing to do with the game:  The expansion support sucks right now. (this was written March 2010)

Goblin Skirmishers?  OOP.  Goblin Marauders? OOP.  Dwarven Battalion?  OOP.  Hundred Years War?  OOP.  Scottish Wars ...very soon to be OOP.

What will we have?  $15-$26 for 1-10 singular plastic figurines by fantasy flight games.  

The last I checked, this was a richard borg wargame, and not heroscape.  

All this wouldn't be bad, and I'd cross my fingers and hope for more in the future...but I guess the guy from fantasy flight games said, "don't hold your breath on the reprints" ...and it seems like they're focusing on battle of westeros more than anything right now.

Really, really disappointing.  Because I think the special races of armies were the best thing this game had in it...  better than dragons, creatures, or heroes.  ...and it seems like less than half the support memoir '44 gets.

04-25-10:  Ok, in hindsight...foresight...whatever.  Fantasy flight appears to give a hoot about this.  They're doing some good stuff.  A little overpriced?  Yes.  The right things for this?  I was wrong, and this is indeed a heck yeah.  The dwarves are too 'weak' as far as having a flavor, goblins are good, but the heroes, dragons, and creatures? AWE-SOME...  and they're republishing goblins.

Sold:  Basically it wasn't getting to the table, and I didn't see any reason why it would be getting to the table any time soon.

RATING: 7
(BGG, you better not mess up my paragraph breaks! :angry: )

Helll Yeeeah! I love the card driven choices. I love whoopin up a good combo of lore and command cards. I love to take out that last little dude in your unit. I love to be BOLD!

Strategies to work on for next time - Let no single hp unit escape. Secure those VPs. No time limit, so mainly hit enemy where he can't hit back (flanks, scouts, runts). Ensure own troops can always "battle back". Ensure clear paths of retreat. Get behind enemy units to turn retreats into kills. Use Lore cards that turn lore into hits. Use more lore cards to extend movement. consider how cheap lore cards may be employed regularly.

Learn the command card deck. Study command cards and lore cards prior to playing. Use knowledge of cards, their quality and quantity to advantage. Use knowledge of quantity of cards to assess availability of certain cards in order to correctly judge viability of thrust into certain areas. Know strong cards and save for best opportunity. Use "BattleLore" card to get deck shuffled and reset card availability.Cavalry and Archers can make good use of range so put them on left/center and center/right hexes.

Green guys have weak attacks already so station them in trees. Mobile green guys can get into range quickly, so only use for quick finishing attacks.

Order of general destruction:
Weakened, unsupported, non-bold red unit.
Weakened, unsupported, non-bold blue unit.
Weakened, unsupported, non-bold green unit.
unsupported, non-bold red unit.
unsupported, non-bold blue unit.
unsupported, non-bold green unit.

Order of final destruction (end game = a one turn win):
Weakened, unsupported, non-bold red unit.
Weakened, unsupported, non-bold blue unit.
Weakened, unsupported, non-bold green unit.
unsupported, non-bold red unit.
unsupported, non-bold blue unit.
unsupported, non-bold green unit.
supported, bold green unit.
supported, bold blue unit.
supported, bold red unit.

Attack weakened units first, then attack normal units. Avoid attacking Bold units unless they have weak attack (green or terrain hampered). Make use of mobile units (mounted, rush) for finishing blows. Use archers to push back closing units.

Alan Paull says: "...In most turns you will only move two to four units, and you might cast a spell every two or three turns. This means that you can concentrate on your broad strategy, how you will deploy your forces to achieve the tactical effect you want. There is strategic skill in where you decide to concentrate your forces, although implementation of your strategic plan will depend on the command cards at your disposal. ...best to play a poor card just to get it out of your hand..."

Yes, get it out of your hand and make small adjustments to your lines and setup. Try to create a good set of complementary cards to make a sustained push along with some lore. 3 or 4 good push actions, combined with some Lore power each time, should be all it takes to win any game of BattleLore.

Rule Clarifications -
* terrain modifers only affect base dice
* a retreating unit loses it's bold status during a battle, even if it retreats into a supported position
* a non-supported unit can be made suddenly bold if a retreating unit causes it to be supported.
* ignoring a flag = bold, bold = ignoring a flag

Latest tactics to try when opportunity arises:
* Use weaker faster units (goblin cav) for chasing and running down lone weakened, unsupported, and frightened units.
* Don't present opportunity for opponent to run down weakened units, obscure them behind stronger units or else move them into supported positions so they can at least offer battle back.
* Establish supported strong units in important locations (strong positions) so as to ensure powerful battlebacks and protect against lack of cards.

Weak hand Cards/Strong hand Cards - See cards in terms of suppport in hand. A lone section card is weak if it is unrelated to a plannable sequence, even if it is an individually strong card on its own. Strong hands are made up of multiple related cards that will allow multi-turn action sequences. Consider using weak hand cards to shore up a strong position just prior to executing a multi-turn sequence of strong hand cards.

A strong position is a supported position, with clear retreat paths that provokes the opponent into an ubalanced battle where you roll more dice than your opponent.

Rules to remember:
* At end of turn, first draw command card, then draw lore
* Lore cards played "alongside your command card" are activated BEFORE ordering.

RATING: 10
Richard Borg and Days of Wonder pool the knowledge gained from their experience with Memoir: '44 and pull out all the stops in producing a truly fantatstic and lavish sequel to the Memoir' System. For the uninitiated, the game is about clashing medieval/early-renaissance/fantasy armies and using might and magic in careful combination to win battles. This is accomplished through careful (and sometimes frustrating) card-play which reflect both command/control problems and the fog-of-war. What really sets the game apart from Memoir and similar games is the use of Lore (which is collected from a missed throw if a special face is also rolled on a die). Collect enough Lore and your Wizard (or Rogue, Cleric, Commander, or Warrior) can perform battlefield wonders to help even the score. Another introduction to the system are Creatures which also have fantatstic, Lore-related abilities. Finally, DOW puts the game in a bundle where all the assembly is pretty much done for you. Figures are ready to play with and stickers for the banners already applied. The rules are 80  pages long, but are straightforward and easy to learn. This is because careful language, symbology, and examples (visual and written) are used to explain every rule.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Excellent game, lacks the sheer beautiful simplicity of Memoir, but neither is it overly complex.  The complexity that does exist isn't overwhelming, and adds a depth and variability not present in Memoir.  The expandability of the game is obvious and I feel there is a huge amount of room to move, without making the game a mix/match of a myriad of little 'exceptions to the rule' like many other miniatures games.  

With the Call to Arms expansion, the Epic expansion, and after i have played more i expect the rating to rise.

I dislike the set-up and take-down time of this game.  That is one of the only real detractors for me.  Still, it is not as long as many of the miniatures wargames i used to play.

update:  I bumped up the rating again.  I really enjoy this game with full lore rules, creatures and the Call to Arms expansion.  The game is great fun, and can be significantly taliored from one game to the next so that each feels very individual.  The lore council is fantastic. What I want to see now is a 'setting' behind the game world!!

Update:  I am really enjoying playing this game.  The addition of the Battle Savvy rules is fantastic and the game really sings.  I love the different situations and thought processes you go through playing this game.  The luck of the dice and the neat combos with Lore and Command cards, heroes and creatures.  This is becoming a real favourite of mine - a wonderful, immersive and fun game.

Update: This has really become one of my all time favourite games.  I love the thought that goes into the game, as well as the play of the cards and the luck of the dice.  A really excellent game - especially when combined with the expansions.  To me Battle Savvy is a must play rule that adds to the game (see my comment for the Heroes expansion for more on this).

Battlelore is a wonderful game experience.  I love playing with the different elements of the game - from the War Council - with it's influence over the level of control you have over the battlefield (command cards) to the lore cards.  From the various units, unit abilities, weapon types, terrain influences and so forth.

I used to play a lot of table top minis games, I really like the tight and balanced feeling that Battlelore has, while it manages to bring the flavour of such table top games as well.

The game looks brilliant all set up.  The design of the system is thoroughly interesting and excellent.  A great game I really look forward to playing.

UPDATE: My patience with BattleLore has now officially run out. The basic problem with this game is the eternity it takes to set up. I think the game is OK, but the last time I played it took, like, 30 minutes to set up and tear down a 40 minute game. No thanks. I'm going back to M'44 and C&C:A.
---
UPDATE: I've lowered by rating, becuase after the first flush of play, we really haven't had much desire to go back to BattleLore. It's fun filler, but it's just too generic, too bland, and the scenarios are too samey. I forsee playing it again, but I really hope the Call to Arms expansion can give the game some range (I admit to some skepticism). I have little interest in the Epic version, and am not sure about the specialists.

Sorry about the excessively long comment.
---

I've played half-a-dozen times, most of the scenarios and all the rules (including DIY war councils).

The core medieval system seems to work pretty well. The core game lacks the variety and flavor of Command & Colors: Ancients, but it's also simpler and cleaner-playing (no clunky leaders). I have some concern that the advantages of support might be a little too strong; since all C&C scenarios are basically meeting engagements, you can't be rewarding the defender too much. But, overall it's hard to tell, and I don't yet seriously suspect that the balance is at all off. Regardless, the core system is solid, but if all you want is C&C: Medieval, and you aren't interested in the Fantasy element, you can safely save your $70.

The Lore system seems to work fairly well too. It seems to add a fun Magic: the Gathering-esque element to the game without some of the frustrating limitations of the Command cards. And the more action you take, the more Lore you generate, which is a nice (if subtle) impetus to do stuff. I have some minor qualms that the out-of-character penalty is a bit extreme and can randomly hose you for drawing the wrong cards, but it's not too bad.

Scenarios have been a big bugbear for previous incarnations of the system; it's a good-news/bad-news thing for BatteLore on that count. All the scenarios I've played have seemed reasonably well-balanced (by the standards of C&C:Ancients and Memoir '44, which are admittedly quite miserable). On the other hand, you only get 10 of them, and they seem to lack range - balance seems to have been ensured by giving both players more or less the same forces and then lining them up on fairly sparse battlefields. Most of scenarios I've played all felt awfully samey, and there is little if anything to distinguish the French-like and English-like forces. Two scenarios I played had terrain objectives which, in the best C&C tradition, played no role whatsoever in the game.

It's interesting to note just how different the three C&C games are (Memoir '44, BattleLore, and Command & Colors: Ancients). All three have very different feels to them, even if you discount the magic in BattleLore. Owning one need not preclude the others, and not liking one should not deter you from at least trying (and not pre-judging) the others.

So why only 7? So far, I'm inclined to say BattleLore is a little soulless. The battles have "historical" intro writeups, but they're short and opaque and I really couldn't figure out what the heck was supposed to be going on most of the time. The Lore cards are cool, but there is a fair amount of overlap between the classes (a number of cards do basically or exactly the same thing, just with different titles), and the lack of focus - most of the time you'll get access to most of the classes, it seems - gives it an all-over-the-map sort of feel. My first War Council game, I hired a 2nd level Rogue and never saw a Rogue lore card. A later game saw me with no Rogue, but drawing nothing but Rogue cards.

Also, the setup time for this game is a little over the top. Between setting out the terrain tiles, pulling the figs, finding the right banners, and assembling the Lore deck, in can take 15 minutes to set up a 45-60 minute game. It's not a show-stopper for me, but no question it's a little out of hand.

I'm also a little worried by terminology. "Bold" is not used consistently (sometimes a Lore or Terrain effect will just say "ignore a flag and may battle back") and how "Bold" or "Bold"-equivalent effects stack is murky. And card wording could be clearer. The "Speed" card and its several variants all say a unit can "move one extra hex and still battle". How does that affect medium infantry, which can move one hex and battle or two hexes and not battle? It was really not clear to me if they can move two and battle, or three and battle, or three and not battle, or what. These sort of execution details are really important for a game of this sort.

Overall, I've enjoyed the game. But I think BattleLore's long-term (and even medium-term) replaybility is going to hinge on the expansions: a) giving us a wider and more interesting range of scenarios and terrain; b) having an interesting range of troop types; c) giving the class/Lore system a little more focus; and d) giving us a more coherent game-world. It's cool that Days of Wonder is being ambitious here, and I'd like to see them pull it off. But I wish that the actual BattleLore box showed off the system's potential a little better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The fourth in the Richard Borg card-driven series. The (very) ancient theme juxtaposed against magic and fantastical creatures is a nice mix. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I almost skipped this one thinking it was nothing more than a rethemed Memoir '44. I'm so glad I didn't. It adds a lot to the system while without sacrificing Memoir's light, rapid fee. The Lore system is obviously the biggest difference. But even smaller things like the morale system add some much-needed depth to the game. And the scenarios also feel a lot more balanced in general.

Lore is a wonderful addition. It adds some chaos, sure, but I'll take some spicy chaos vs. Memoir's dry feel ANY day.

The game really starts to shine when you start experimenting with the War Council. This is a really fun way to add replayability and experimentation to the game - something many other combat games lack.

So what's not to like? Well, for starters this game needs an overall rules summary somewhere. If you haven't played in awhile and just want to refresh, it's such a hassle to sift through 80 pages. Barf.

And while the system feels pretty lean, there's still a few fiddly bits. Terrain and weapon effects, some of the rules regarding Creatures... just lots of small things that are easy to forget and don't really add enough to justify their existence.

And, as with other games in the series, it still feels weird/unintuitive that it's just as easy to hit the stronger units as it is to hit the weak ones. Not a huge deal in THIS version of C&C as there's Lore to balance things out a bit, but it does mean the die is mostly made up of misses.

Finally - the setup time is a monster. Everything from the design of the game (the reliance on scenario play being a big one) to the production quality (organizing all those minis. ughhhh) affects it, and though I like the game I've been known to turn it to down just because I don't feel like investing in the setup/teardown.

All in all, my favorite of the C&C games. There's ways it could be improved but there's some uniquely fun and interesting mechanics here and the pace of the game is just right.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
My criterion for rating games


http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/147053/item/2300390#item2300390


1)Sense of wonder: a great deal of variety in the game through the unit powers, different scenarios, lore council, the command cards, and the dice. No scenario would ever play the same twice. Fantasy is my favorite theme. I always feel so into the game when I play a fantasy game.

2) Meaningful/fun decisions. Full of decisions from beginning to end on how to play cards, how to move troops, how to use lore and lore cards, and whether to get lore or lore cards. Even if you fall far behind a couple of lucky rolls and a great lore card can turn the whole battle around.

3) Player interaction: You are killing each other. Pretty much the best kind of interaction! :)

4) Components/artwork: Tremendous miniatures and the artwork on everything is amazing. Its Days of wonder and I love their art style.

5) Game length/complexity: Perfect game length. Game setup is way too long with the lore and the call to arms, but the scenarios play out very quickly and can be reset to be played again very quickly. In a 3 hour session you can play a scenario 3-4 times with different setups.

RATING: 7.5
This game has earned my 10 rating.  I like everything about it.  Problem is, I keep getting so many new games, and now I have become significantly more busy at work, that I haven't been able to play for quite some time.  However, it is still probably one of the best games I have ever played!

I will still rank this game a 10, because I think it is that good.  However, the set-up and break-down time of this game can get overwhelming, and takes up almost as much time as actually playing the darn game!  To really get the best out of this game, I recommend that you play at least 2 scenarios each time you bring out the box.

I dropped this rating to a 9 for a few reasons.  First, there are other games that I would like to play more often.  Secondly, there are other games out there that are better (according to the Geek, there are 35+ games that are better...).  I have many of those games, and agree.  Third, I think that for me, there has to be a finite number of games that I can rate a "10" because I can't play everything all the time.  So, I decided that of my "10" games, this one has to go down.

The only two games rated lower on the Geek that I still give a "10" to is Descent and Tide of Iron.  And that is because I would rather play ToI over BattleLore if presented with the choice because I like the theme better.  And Descent fills a different niche.

But BattleLore is still a great game, and a blast to play.  I just don't want to play it as much as I used to because of so many other games out there that I want to play.  Get it?  Good!

EDIT:  Occasionally, I will go through my list of games that I have rated and commented, and make changes and edits as my game tastes change.  About two years ago, I dropped this to a 7.5   I like the game and the "C&C System".  But after many plays against the same opponent (who moved away, got married, and had a son over the past 5 years), this game has sat in my closet, buried under other stuff that will get played with my wife more often.  So I have not played in a very long time, and although I am disappointed by that (because I really like the game), I get the impression that I will never play it again, despite how much I actually like it.  And that makes me sad, but I am okay with that.  Such is the life of a game collector who has lots and lots of stuff, but can't play everything all of the time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
First off, a little editorial.  When I first opened up Battlelore I have to admit that I was very, very underwhelmed and even a little disappointed.  Sure, the graphic design and artwork is pretty close to best-in-the-business and the seasoned Commands and Colors system promised a great game out of the box but my initial response was that the $70 retail price tag was outrageous for a game that included no more components and nothing quality-wise beyond what we've come to expect from a $50 DoW or FFG game.  By all appearances the exaggerated pricetag went to help DoW pull out all the stops in making this one of the most accessible and idiot-proof games of its class- all the stickers are applied and many figures are already flagged, every counter and terrain tile is punched out, there's two of every reference card, and the rulebook is almost obsessively thorough (i'm surprised it doesn't have a diagram showing you how to hold the dice).  This is great and all, but really, who is going to buy a $70 board game that isn't already predisposed to it or that doesn't already have experience with games of this type?  I'd rather all those production dollars put toward making the game "friendly" had been spent on better quality miniatures, more diverse units out-of-the-box, or cutting the cards so they could fit in card sleeves.  On top of that, the game feels incomplete out of the box- the continual promises of more content, more units, and more scenarios throughout the rulebook and promotional materials feels like a cheap come-on and for $70 I expected something a little more comprehensive (like C&C:A or even Memoir '44). I do like that it's a system, and I do expect it to expand in some interesting ways (hopefully including scenarios with victory conditions other than unit elimination), but right now the game is really just a tease for greatness to come.Which is the problem.  After 6 months of play and the first expansion, I don't see any sign that BATTLELORE will be nearly the "evolutionary" experience it's been marketed as and the initial rush of such a beautifully produced game with a good solid system has worn off and all that's left is a sense of "that's it?"  The tactical mechanics of the game have revealed themselves time and time again to completely fail to appear anything like 100 Years War-era warfare (can you believe how underpowered the archers and the longbow are?) and the extended setup/breakdown time pretty much limits play- there's no reason to spend 30 minutes to set up a game of this relative depth.  It's one thing to set up TI3 or DESCENT for a great evening of deep gameplay but it's another to spend as much time putting these stupid figures (which amount to nothing more than poker chips or counters) into formation.  I like the C&C system a lot, but if I want to play it I find myself wishing to play ANCIENTS over BATTLELORE for any number of reasons, including better command cards, more interesting strategic possibilities, and better integration of the theme into the system.  BATTLELORE is _certainly_ not a bad game at all and there's plenty to recommend it but I don't think any number of supplemental add-ons are going to make it into anything more than it already is.  The CALL TO ARMS expansion is almost completely disposable and should have been included in the base game to begin with.

RATING: 7
Quite good light tactical war game. Skill v luck ratio is about 50:50. The control of the flow of the battle is bit too limited to my taste. I especially dislike retreat rules: retreating units can often maneuver to a better position. (rating down by 1) The short length is a big plus, unfortunately the setup is exceedingly long. However, the set-up can be shortened considerably with 1) proper organization of standard bearers; and 2) using any type of footmen/cavalry figures for other members of the unit, e.g. using any type of human sword or bow to represent additional hit points of a blue sword unit.  Also, mercenaries seem to be unbalanced  in the basic set: goblins are much weaker than dwarfs. Scenarios do not compensate for it sufficiently.
 
The game looks great (if not overproduced), especially the rulebook (rating up by 1). Although I have not played enough (maybe 20 or so games) I have a nagging feeling that the game does not have much replay value in long term. Maybe a few expansions would help this.

RATING: 10
I bought this when it first came out for about $60.  I had been reading about it before it became available.  This is my all-time favourite game.  I have bought all of its expansions.
This game ranks at the very top of several categories for me (in no particular order):
1. Value per weight.  The box weighs about 5 pounds without any of the figures in it.
2. Most components per dollar.  The base game came with 210 miniatures!  I only tried to fit them back in the box a few times, now I keep them in storage containers stacked 6 trays high.  With the dozen expansions I have, those trays are full of figures.
3. Replayability.  The Lore cards add great replayability, let alone all of the scenarios and specializations available.  The Call to Arms expansion adds additional replayability.  And then there are a thousand additional unofficial scenarios available online.
4. I love the theme of medieval warfare.  That's what this game provides.
5. Not counting initial setup and takedown, a complete game can be played in 1 hour.  Trade sides and try again, or move on to the next scenario.
6. The production value offered by Days of Wonder is amazing.  The 80-page full colour rule book is the best I have ever seen.  The cards and components are amazing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I'll be looking hard at the forthcoming 2nd Edition.  Maybe an upgrade will be in order.

RATING: 7
Yeah, the tactical model suffers from the I-can’t-do-anything-because-I-don’t-have-a-card-for-that-flank syndrome that plagues this genre, but what I couldn’t stand in Memoir ‘44 I cheerfully accept in a fantasy milieu.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The Lore cards add a chaotic new dimension to the game which has both benefits and drawbacks, adding some neat tricks but increasing the unpredictability of combat.  The move to tie Battle Back to Bold makes for a more aggressive game - it's not enough to sit back and let the enemy come to you.  The reduction of retreats to 1 hex per flag for all normal units really increases the utility of cavalry over those in C&C:A.  Complaints: The deck of reference cards is immensely annoying, scattering important rules and interactions over several cards.  The minis are fiddly to move and hard to tell apart on the board and, contrary to the sculpts included in the base set, medieval armies are not made up entirely of Level 1 Fighters with short sword and shield.  The unit symbols on the dice and flags really should have been distinguished by more than just color - didn't DoW learn anything from the TrR board/cards? All in all, I like it less than C&C:A but more than M44.  It's not something I'm going to buy, but I'm glad I have a few friends who own it.

Update: 8 -> 6 and 9.  Why both?  This game is a 6 out of the box.  No truly full scenarios and no instructions for some of the units it comes with, and a month later nothing has changed.  But this game is a 9 the way I play it.  Made my own army creation rules, my own terrain creation rules, my own monster rules, my own extra terrain type rules... It's all so very good.  But I don't want to mislead someone looking to buy the game since I don't come in the box.  6 for you, 9 for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players play out battles on a large hex playing board, ordering their units via command cards.  Strange fantasy themed game sees medieval style battles fought out during the 100 Years War, of course with goblins, wizards, dwarves and giant spiders lending their support (the chronicles must have left that last bit out).  The next game in the Battle Cry series, and it strongly resembles the most recent release, Command & Colors: Ancients by GMT.  Players on their turn play a command card, which dictates what type/flank of troops to activate.  Activated troops can move and/or battle.  Battles are rolling a bunch of dice and counting matching hits.  When the last troop figure in a hex is eliminated, that counts as a victory banner for the winner of the battle, and banners determine who wins.  It's a fun system, doesn't require  too much thinking (although I haven't won a game with this system in ages), just slap down cards and rumble in with the troops.  Rules are semi-programmed, adding in fantasy creatures, Lore (magic), etc.  Certainly looks very nice, although the box is a bit flimsy considering the amount of stuff that was packed in it.  Lovely, lavish rulebook.  Looking forwards to seeing where this game system goes (I'm sure there'll be more!).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
So finally Days of Wonder delivers something that people have always wanted: a fantasy-medieval boardgame. None of the other titles like Warhammer Fantasy or Wizard Kings had the accessibility or quality fill this niche. So this was a long time in coming! 

System-wise Borg has improved the general rules for morale and also done a great job of differentiating unit types in an elegant manner.

Regarding the production, Days of Wonder again aims too high and gets snagged. Lots of production issues should have been fixed: soft plasics are inferior for paints and they even warped. Low-quality dice, lack of a follow-on product, and too few scanarios in the base game also add to the woes. I applaud their ambition, but they clearly have problems executing their production vision.

I prefer the rules to C&C:Ancients because of the use of leaders, a better command deck, and widespread battle-back.

UPDATE Dec 2013:

I went as far as helping Universal Head bring his BL player aid up to date just so I had a chance at keeping the units straight, but it was still a hassle.  When BL 2nd Edition came out, I realized that was closer to what i wanted to get out of this game, so BL1 was sold.

RATING: 7
Fun game with some problems. The following comments should be understood as a comparison with other games using the C&C system:

The positives are the Lore system and the excellent range of possible expansions.

The negatives are the monochrome plastic pieces (it can be really difficult to tell which pieces are on which side) and the lack of any historical rationale even for the "historical" scenarios (medieval warfare with no lances or pikes?) Components are generally overwrought. Set-up time can be absurdly long. The rulebook is so massive as to make it almost useless as a reference.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
No doubt the best of the C&C system so far. I've been partial to both M'44 and Ancients from their inception and I see BL as more of an evolution than the same game with different clothes.

I am firmly in the camp that loves games that expand and grow. BL is designed for that and in a smoother fashion than M'44 and Ancients. Here we will be able to pick and choose how we want to craft our BL system as opposed to the otheres where we pretty much have to grab the entire expansion or nothing. Either way is fine by me but I look at BL as an elevation in planning and production.

Not only does this game play as smoothly and tensely as it's older brethren, it also looks fantastic when in use. I already knew I'd like the game but until the second play it hadn't occured to me that part of the warm glow I was feeling was due to the visuals and heft of the components in use. A great job. Unless you hate, hate, hate a random element in your games or hate, hate, hate fantasy, you'll probably enjoy this stellar game.

RATING: 7
The fantasy theme works well with the C&C system - Freed from historicity, the C&C system doesn't come under as much scrutiny here. The scenarios also appear better balanced compared to Memoir'44 and C&C:A. No 6-0, 6-1 hosings here. The lore aspect gives players more tactical options, which goes some way to ameliorating the "screwed by card draw" problem. 

While the French/English battles are ok, I think things get really interesting with the goblins. The bold/frightened aspect of the goblins makes things really tense. The goblin army can appear strong but can also crumble really fast! 

It's still not something I would buy though. The price is high - $70, not something I can justify with such a light game, and for only two players. I'm immediately drawn to comparison with Clash of the Gladiators, which is cheaper, takes up to 5 players and offers good decisionmaking and dice-rolling fun. Of course, CotG doesn't have nice minis like BattleLore, but I can live with that.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Played 10+ times with 2p.
Ratings history: 8|8|8|8|8....

[b]Another Borg light-wargame, but this one comes with spells. The base game would have been better with more monsters, but a solid little wargame I'm happy to play. [i]I would trade this if the right deal came along.[/i][/b]

:thumbsup: PROS:
- Colorful, gorgeous, good minis (and lots of them)
- Reference cards make game a bit easier to learn
- Spells are strong, but fun
- One of the stronger Richard Borg war games
- Great instruction booklet
- Campaign introduces rule complexity a bit at a time
- Lots of fan-made maps out there
- different levels of each troop type is genius

:thumbsdown: CONS:
- Base game only comes with one monster
- Expansions are expensive
- Ho-hum, another Richard Borg wargame
- Lots of little rules new players will struggle with
- I want my side of the board to be one color (color system a bit counter-intuitive)
- Some spells are STRONG and can wipe an opponent

Gorgeous game with tons of minis, spell cards, etc. Tons of (overpriced) expansions too! I rediscovered my enjoyment of BattleLore (1st ed.) this week, which was nice because I've been trying to sell it (at a massive loss), and now will keep it as I enjoy it more than the loss I would take by selling. Borg's C&C with spells and nice graphics works well. I do wish expansions were more interesting, and cheaper, but with a wealth of fan-made scenarios out there, I'm sure I'll have years of fun with this.

RATING: 9
After years of saying I wouldn't, I caved.  The shocking part: my wife is not only on board with this, it was sort of her idea.  She likes it so far, after two plays.  I'm a pretty experienced Memoir '44 and C&C: Ancients player, and it's definitely what you'd expect if you've played those games. For me, the reasons to play BL as opposed to other games in the series are the War Council and Lore, which really elevate the game into something different from the rest. Creatures are cool, but can have little impact on the game, depending upon how well they roll.

My least favorite thing about BL: the scenarios included are way too gradual about introducing all of the cool concepts. I get that they wanted the game to be easily accessible for newer gamers. But for C&C veterans like myself, it's frustrating. I've started jumping right in with the later scenarios, assuming that eventually we'll be using Call To Arms pretty much every time we play.

For a game that I've barely played, my wife and I have accumulated expansions at an alarming rate. In fact, we are now only missing [thing=45543]Heroes[/thing] and [thing=30232]Hundred Years' War[/thing]. I've decided I can live without both, considering the pricing and availability issues.

RATING: 6
I picked this up to play with my son.  I like the Command and Colors system best with C&C Ancients but am going to give Battlelore a try....  

I've played quite a few times and Battlore is very fun.  All the C&C system games really boil down to light card games with a touch of hand management (I'm up in the air on how much hand management matters).  Luck of the draw and dice are very strong in these short battles.  The C&C system is lots of fun however and Battlore is a fantastic box-o-toys.  This is a great intro wargame and a perfect father vs kid game.  Just introduce it in stages and don't sweat all the details.
 I haven't really jumped into the lore system so far because it seems kind of tedious and the rulebook is so wordy but I'm enjoying the game without it.

Update:  I've cooled on Battlelore.  I like the base game but the Lore system is too much work.  I'd play C&C Ancients instead.

RATING: 8
Easy to learn and play, everyone I know seems to like it regardless of how much they play board games, and there's lots of interesting strategy in it, no 2 games play alike.

EDIT:  After repeated plays, the long set up times combined with the chaotic gameplay does eventually start to get old.  Also, many of the expansions are pretty lackluster, and in particular the new paper map / troll expansion is a big step in the wrong direction.  I still love this game, and it will still come out, but not as often, and I doubt I will buy much in the way of expansions.

EDIT:  And now it's been bought by FFG - hopefully this means good things!  I doubt my rating will rise back to 10 again though - I've moved on to deeper wargames like Conflict of Heroes, and while I credit Battlelore as being the game that brought me into wargames, I don't see myself playing this anywhere nearly as much as I used to.

RATING: 7
I really loved this game, but the system and how it is handled sucks. Many years have passed since the release of the core game and still there are no new races (UNDEAD!). FFG re-release the old goblin, dwarf and human figures instead of giving the players something really fresh to get excited about.

The Call To Arms expansion was particularly disappointing. It prolonged the set up time significantly and was just a random army generation system that doesn't give players enough control. Also, due to some specialist cards, it made some units useless and others necessary to win (mounted knights). The weaker units didn't make it to table at all outside the provided scenarios. 

This game desperately needs new races, new magic deck and a huge scenario/campaign book. It also needs the army creation rules to be revisited and tweaked.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A great game I would highly recommend for gateway gamers looking for an easy-to-learn conflict-type game. It looks good, plays great, and is easy to learn and understand. The chrome will get non-gamers interested right away, but the price tag may frighten them off, as well as the sheer size of the rulebook.

The biggest downfall would be the set-up (which, if done efficiently, will still take upwards of 30 mins or more), but once you're set-up, it's easy to play several scenarios at once. The medeival scenarios are quick, but once Lore enters the picture, and choosing a War Council, things have a tendency of slowing down even more during set-up.

The gameplay itself is rather fast and furious (unless you're playing with some one whom takes 20 minutes to decide which single unit they wish to move), and rather luck-based. If you're a big strategy war-gamer, stay the hell away from BattleLore, as you'll likely just be disappointed.

However, if you're up for a quick, easy battle game, go for it. This one works well with children too, as the oodles of minis, and ease of play make it super-cool for the kids.

I've since lowered my rating as the set up time is ridiculous. It's been years since I've played and it's doubtful I will again - 30 minutes to over an hour to set up is far too long for this family.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
The system is solid but the scenarios are "samey" and the whole Hundred Years War With Giant Spiders background just doesn't cut it for me. I committed deeply to the game, buying several expansions and painting them all, but over time this title has slipped in my estimations and just doesn't get played so often as the other titles in the series. 

There seems a bit less maneuver here than in C&C: Ancients (the obvious measuring stick for this game); rather than advancing in line your units have a tendency to break into three or more mutually-supporting elements, and there they stay as they fight and die. I think terrain is a bit better handled here than in CCA, but I do miss leaders and evasion. Missile units have longer range here, and thus can do more damage over time -- you don't have the mad scramble of running your lights up to missile range and only getting off one volley before the enemy line crunches back into you. There seem to be fewer retreats in this game, but the odds are no different ... it must be due to an increased attention paid to keeping your units in supporting position, to ensure they remain "bold" to both resist retreat and earn the all-important ability to battle back. Likewise, you find yourself hunting for those unsupported flank units that can't hit back. 

Overall, this game feels as "medieval" to me as CCA felt "ancient" ... it's not a simulation, but in the aggregate it feels as authentic as any of the substantially more complex medieval wargames that I have played. And experiencing this game has caused me to even better appreciate what a wonderful game CCA is, as well. I'm happy to have both in my library, but I'd like this game a lot more (and play it a lot more) if the background world wasn't such a pratfall.

http://agapintheabuse.blogspot.com/2007/02/back-to-battlelore.html#links

RATING: 8
This is a good game.  Played with the basic rules, which means no magic.  It's medieval combat, using the COMMANDS & COLORS: ANCIENT system, with a few minor changes.  DoW did an awesome job with the pieces, as they are made of sturdy yet flexible plastic which is perfect for paint.


The rest of the production is of top quality, from the full color perfect bound 80 page rulebook, the scenario book, the counters, the plastic playing pieces and the double sided board (one side is divided for "Overlord" type setup, so two boards would have 3 sections as opposed to 6, for those of us who like epic battles!).


As opposed to GMT's approach of using hardwood blocks, DoW went with generic units which all have a socket for a flag.  One differentiates the units with flags, one flag per 3-4 pieces, depending on type.  Of course there are light, medium and heavy Infantry and Cavalry, along with archers.  Long swords and short swords, bow and crossbows.


Definitely a must have for those who enjoyed C&C:A and like the Medieval period, as well as those looking for a more challenging game than M'44.  Highly recommended!

RATING: 8
I love Battlelore (first edition).  I just never get to play it anymore because its only two player and it takes forever to set up.  Most of my 2-player game time is dedicated to lunch breaks or quick hits before game night with my gaming group. 

The actual gameplay of Battlelore is exceptional under the Command and Colors mechanic.  It really plays to the strengths of strategic thinkers while throwing in just enough randomness to give it an edge.  If there wasn't any dice and every movement resulted in exact results the game would lose a lot of it's depth.  I really, really enjoy the fact that at any given time an army's line can be "broken" and all heck can break loose between the two sides.

Throw in the promo pieces from the pre-launch sales and there is a lot of variety to be had here.  This will be a keeper and an heirloom since it is no longer being printed and the 2nd edition is being released.

RATING: 2
6-28-10
Yearly comment update - I still don't see myself ever trying this game again.  I've never been fond of 1v1 games, especially direct conflict games, think the theme is very thin and uninteresting, and found nothing redeemable about the gameplay but the art.  Below old comments still apply.

I wasn't particularly excited to play a 1v1 game, Maybe a 2vM game but 1v1 has never appealed to me.  I gave it a try though, with my group telling me it's a fun, strategic game.  We set it up, got everything ready and started playing.  I realized I made a lot of bad unit card choices and really wasn't going to win until my next play.  

However, it came time and I played a cavalry card.  I did some fair damage and was happy with the turn.  Then my opponent played a counter-card, to mimic my action.  His cavalry (as he had more than I did) stomped all over my troops.  I tried to recover, and his next turn he played the same cavalry card that I initially played, stomping even further.  He had his 8 kills to my 1 and the game was done.  Any combination that powerful, with the luck involved and my lack of interest with the game, just totally turned me off on this one.  I was extremely annoyed and will never play this again.

RATING: 6
Jan 5, 2008 - first game with Scenario 1.  It took us 1,5 h to finish it while reading the rules as we go.  With each and every minute the game became more interesting, especially when we figured that units may retreat, battle back, pursue, advance to emptied space, support, cannot use ranged attack while engaged, etc.  We still have lots to learn: lore, terrain and other rules.  I plan to go slowly with this game and complete each and every of the introductory scenarios, although I am tempted to jump in to Lore battles right away.

Jan 7, 2008 - I started painting the minis.  I hate all that gray on the board.  It is totally uninspiring and all the details (faces, weaponry, texture) are lost.  Painted figures seem to have "personality".

Jan 10, 2008 - Today, we played with the simple lore rules for the first time. The gameplay becomes somewhat less strategic but is faster and allows for card combos.  Nice addition.  +0.5 in ranking. Now, we have two modes to play this game: medieval rules and lore.  It gives more flexibility and increases replayability.

Jan 23 - I like the game a lot.  I have decided to buy all the expansions. 

Feb. 18 - increased rating by 0.5 points due to expansions.  They bring a lot into the game.  Deployment rules with Call to Arms, new units with specialists expansions.   I can see bright future ahead of BattleLore - there is so much DoW can do to further refine and enrich the game.

Setup time kills this game - it takes forever.

BATTLE SAVVY TROOPS
Battle Savvy troops are veteran fighters that
understand the strike and counter strike of combat.
Battle Savvy units have the following rules:
• A Battle Savvy unit attacked in melee combat
that is not eliminated or forced to retreat from its
hex may battle back.
• A retreating Battle Savvy unit may not battle
back even if the retreat places the unit in a hex
that is adjacent to the attacking unit.
• When a Battle Savvy unit in melee is unable
to fulfill a required retreat movement, the unit
may battle back (after it takes losses and if it is
still in its original hex).
• Battle Savvy units with ranged weapons do
not collect Lore tokens in ranged combat.
• Battle Savvy units with ranged weapons
collect Lore tokens in melee combat.
• Battle Savvy ranged weapon troops and other
units that do not normally score a hit when a is
rolled in melee still force a casualty check against
a Champion when one or more symbols are
rolled.

RATING: 9
[b]AMAZINGLY FUN, AMAZINGLY FAST LIGHT WARGAME SET IN THE HIGH MIDDLE AGES, WITH THE OCCASIONAL OUTBURST OF MAYHEM AND MAGIC, EVERY NOW AND THEN...[/b]

As a newbie to the whole wargaming area of board games and all-round geekness (BattleLore was my first wargame after just a year or so of starting my affair with board games), BattleLore is a beautiful surprise for anyone interested in starting a wargame section in a board game collection. The best of both worlds, BattleLore sure is a game to be reckoned with.

Other than the fact that the guys at Days of Wonder (I own a DoW copy, not a Fantasy Flight Games copy) really worked hard on the design of the game board, and the bits and pieces, the whole game set is a treat for the eyes, including the game box and the plastic trays. The miniatures, though bendy and somewhat fragile, are beautifully carved and conceived, but painting them must take at least two to three months of normal hobbying time. Nonetheless the game board implicitly invites players to create and build their own 3D scenery to go along with the miniatures (It doesn’t seem to be a too far-fetched dream!). The war council sheets are amazingly painted and the mini board painted on the table of the commander gives a bit of a comical or cartoonish side to the game. And let’s not forget about the scenario booklet, filled with details and with a small background text of each battle. It’s a gorgeous master piece.

The game play is a fast-paced, tension-bringing crucible between the players, both focused on getting as much out of their forces as fast as possible, with the least amount of damage received in each turn. The special actions of each unit, the different kind of weapons and the landscape features really add depth to an already awesome game, with almost no effort at all. Richard Borg really outdid himself with this one. I can’t imagine other game in the Commands & Colours series being better than BattleLore. 
Finally, the rulebook, besides being one of the most clear rulebooks I’ve ever had the pleasure of reading is another artistic achievement, since it combines perfectly the importance of graphic examples and the amount of space for each subject in the game.

BattleLore is a game I don’t think I’ll ever refuse to play. Ever.

[b][i]I. Criteria[/i][/b]
Mechanics: :d10-0:
Design & Art: :d10-0:
Packing & Presentation: :d10-8:
Theme: :d10-9:
BGG Factor: :d10-8:

[b][i]II. Sliders[/i][/b]

Abstract [b]______[COLOR=#FF0000][7][/COLOR]___[/b] Themed: Considering that BattleLore is a re-boot of the Commands & Colours system created by Richard Borg, it seems hard to think it would be too much of a difference if instead of medieval wars, the theme of the game would be space invasions fought between moons and comets, using the same hexed game board. However, it is impossible to conceive a better setting for the game’s expansions than that of the fantasy Middle Ages portrayed in BattleLore, especially since the expansions introduce units with typical European weapons used during those centuries. Fantastic History is way easier to use than Futuristic Science Fiction.

Simple [b]__[COLOR=#FF0000][3][/COLOR]_______[/b] Complex: As mentioned before, the presence of multiple kinds of units, weapons and landscapes instantly gives BattleLore an endless supply of possible movements, actions and military options to go along with those possibilities. Also, the simplicity and “straight forwardness” of the rules makes those options a lot more easy to grasp than expected, since the same restrictions applied to one’s own units, apply to those of the enemy.

Strategic [b]_______[COLOR=#FF0000][8][/COLOR]__[/b] Tactical: The size of the game board has a reason to be, which is to enforce the idea of BattleLore being a light wargame and not a full-fledged classical wargame. This results in the idea that almost every action taken by a single unit will end up most likely affecting the controlling player more sooner than later, positively or negatively. It is hard to think of strategic possibilities in BattleLore without compromising a full flank, or letting some specific enemy unit go unharmed when the opportunity presents itself. However, defensive strategies may be built around protection of slower or weaker units.

Luck [b]___[COLOR=#FF0000][4][/COLOR]______[/b] Skill: For a veteran grognard, BattleLore may hold secrets not available for beginners, so there’s always a particular amount of skill involved in the game, like in the way the units are chosen to attack or in choosing the places the attacks happen and how the enemy reacts to those situations. But the presence of dice and the card driven mechanism of the game turn are too important and significant to be overlooked, since the player cannot act on the board without a card allowing them to do so, and the attacks, no matter how brilliant and carefully planned they are, may end up in nothing if the dice don’t roll on the right sides.

Fiddly [b]________[COLOR=#FF0000][9][/COLOR]_[/b] Elegant: Like I said before, the BattleLore rulebook is one of the best rulebooks I’ve ever read, so there is not much uncertainty on how the rules are applied and how the game progresses. Also, the reference cards given to each player are a crucial element in the game turn and how the tactics and strategies are developed, since those cards show just what the players need for each turn, and the need of using the rulebook is almost non-existing once the players have gone through a few turns each.

Indirect interaction [b]_________[COLOR=#FF0000][10][/COLOR][/b] Direct interaction: Just because this is a wargame, light or not, it should be instantly understood that all interaction between players is direct.

Cutthroat [b][COLOR=#FF0000][1][/COLOR]_________[/b] Friendly: Just because this is a wargame, it should be instantly understood that all interaction between players is cutthroat, since at best, both players are required to eliminate their enemy’s soldiers, wether the scenario imports the need of killing them all or killing enough of them to achieve a certain goal.

Heavy use of language [b]__[COLOR=#FF0000][3][/COLOR]_______[/b] No language needed: Obviously, a card driven wargame needs a lot of language use, in this case, English. But it must be clarified that the cards, or at least most of them, don’t require much reading after a few games of BattleLore. Players end up understanding the very few symbols on them and how they work from the little pictures. The same cannot be said for the reference cards, which feature a lot of language (Too much to be ignored) and some of them also deal with in-game specific terms, like “morale” and “pursuit actions”, to name the easiest ones. Not a game for people that can’t read in English.

[b][i]III. Number of players recommended[/i][/b]: 2 (Duh!).

[b]CHEERS![/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Days of Wonder set out with the goals of "Short playing sessions, strong online community, easy to learn yet deeply engaging game play, superlative artwork and components, and finely tuned game mechanics." Much as some may nitpick and backbite it, DoW has achieved this for my play group. 

The movement and combat rules take a few games to understand, but they're worth struggling with since it's their very quirkyness that adds the "charging knights and slashing swords" feel to the game that makes you want to come back again. In particular the rules "Gaining Ground", "Battle Back", "Pursuit Action", and that calvary get to basicly attack twice and move three times as long as they keep up a string of successful attacks takes a while to do properly.

The basic scenarios even before Lore and Councils are introduced are fun --after you wrap your brain around how movement and combat works. It's also an adjustment that archery is woefully inaccurate and weak --as the scenario "Agincourt" shows! (Although drawing "Darken the Skies" is usually a game winner for the English) After playing two games of the first scenario and one of the second, we skipped to scenario 5 without problem, which is the first Lore scenario.

The Lore scenarios play much faster, more fluidly, and with more comebacks from behind. Scenario 5 is where we found the game system starts to glimmer and shine.

The investment for this title is rather high --$50 from an on-line vendor like http://www.timewellspent.org --but keep in mind you're not buying a single game --you're buying 10 games in one box. That makes the purchase price more reasonable.

RATING: 7
Well, BattleLore didn't disappoint, but it didn't quite play as I had imagined.  I guess I was really expecting it to be significantly different from Memoir, for some reason, which it really isn't.  While the morale mechanic encourages formations and more skillful play, the double shot of luck in cards and dice is just as there as it ever was.  The Lore council is neat, too, and I like the depth in unit types and battle-backs, etc., but I just don't know if I *love* the system enough to play the endless expansion collecting game.  BattleLore is a great game, but maybe I'm just not the target gamer for it after all.  If the victory conditions were more dynamic and less sumplistic, I'd be willing to give it another try.

RATING: 8
Really fun strategy warfare game for 2 players.  I have issues with the setup at times, and must really plan to play this game, however once you begin playing it really does become an epic and thrilling battle.  The expansions are fun, I personally would suggest looking into what comes with the particular set, if you own Warhammer or other miniatures you can often substitute them for expansions and save some money and storage space.  The game play does revolve around dice rolls so luck does have a factor, but placement and choice of attack can also play a major factor, add in Lore cards and the game is deeper than any other of its kind.  The combat is built on the same premise as Memoir '44 so if you enjoyed that, this game adds another layer with the Lore.  I would classify this game as advanced due to the amount of pieces, rules, and variations.

RATING: 5.5
Ideal Players: 2

Description: Medieval war meets sorcery in this two-player fantasy wargame. While similar to Memoir '44, BattleLore adds some theme-appropriate tactics.

Pro: Mechanics match theme. Nice plastic miniatures. Magic and skills add strategic depth. Flanking bonuses reward formations. Simple combat system.

Con: Cards and dice can make battles somewhat random. Plastic figures are fiddly to get out and put away. Plastic figures bend easily. Difficult to tell each side's units apart at-a-glance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
As good as any of the Commands and Colors systems, but your favorite will depend on the theme (Civil War: Battle Cry, WW2: Memoir '44, Ancients: Command and Colors Ancients, Fantasy: Battlelore). I own them all and enjoy them all. 


It's a very fun system that works for many levels of gamers. 

Battlelore has potential be my favorite of the series, but I'm playing it more and it's new. I like the lore addition, the creature additions (they are special units), and am looking forward to how Days of Wonder expands it because I feel the expansions are needed to open the base game up; right now, I feel there could be some more to the Lore as it feels a bit limited, but that's clearly on the way in the expansions.

This game is not collectible in a blind buying sense (meaning: you don't know what you get when you buy a booster), it is expandable and what you buy in a booster is open knowledge and complete. While I'm not excited about a potential money pit, I'm looking forward to the boosters to put more flesh on this system.

Each version of this system has brought more to the table. Days of Wonder has done a wonderful presentation here and has great bits. The game play is solid, the games are fairly easy to teach, the games play in a modern day friendly time period. The fantasy element in Battlelore is well done, the lore possibilities have me intrigued. It's a very fun system.

Do not expect realism, or complexity, the system abstracts things. But if you're after a fun war game, these games are great.

UPDATE: This game is a lot of fun, the production is fantastic, but I still feel the base box--despite the addition of lore--is a bit, incomplete compared to the other base games. It feels like it's waiting for the expansions; as such, I've docked it a point. 

The Command and Colors game system has been a very solid 8 across the board for me and I'd expect Battleore to end up there as well.

RATING: 9
Bought this on spec after owning Memoir '44 for a year.  I refer to it as the 100 years' war with orcs, but that's not really fair---they call them goblins, and it's clear this game is aiming for the "Warhammer Fantasy Battle, only less annoying" niche.  I've enjoyed miniatures in the past, but never really had the patience for all that painting and was pissed off by the continuous figure power inflation that required endless buying and re-buying.  Plus I just don't have the time and space anymore.By contrast BattleLore is much quicker to set up, to play, and to re-play.  And the mechanics allow subtle tactical play and reward careful planning.  The one downside is the amount of time required to set up.  When playing solitaire I tend to ditch the miniatures (which are really glorified hit point markers) in favor of dice, keeping the single marker miniature to track unit type.  Indeed, the ancients rules use wooden blocks to accomplish exactly the same goal.There are quite a few expansions for this now.  I'd love to try them out, but I'd like to see  bigger player pool first!  I'm particularly keen to try the Epic version, which permits a good deal more maneuver before engagement (while compensating by allowing multiple card plays).  And a good campaign game could be quite interesting.

RATING: 5
Apart from minor and fixable components concerns, this takes everything good about M'44 and adds to it (or maybe it's more accurate to say borrows a few things from C&C:A and then adds a few new things). Tactical depth is increased pretty significantly over M'44, but the options aren't daunting (though it will take a few plays, probably, to get the units straight). Only ten scenarios included, but there's the online scenario editor and the new official and fan-made scenarios to consider as well. I like the fact that the game can be both an "ordinary" medieval game and one with magical/fantasy elements in it. Customizable to a fault, this game is going to give players a lot of options, but I think they'll remain that, just options. Putting two boards together for Epic play should be truly epic, and BL wisely goes with a square Epic setup (unlike M'44, which uses a "long" board for its Overlord scenarios). The main problem I see is set-up and take-down, which really do take a while, even with separate bags, etc. Kudos to DoW on this one.

At some level, I think I actually prefer M'44 for its speed of play, historicism, and, well, lack of complication, but BL is at least as good, and it's genuinely richer if that's what you're looking for.

Update: I'm saying goodbye to this one. It's just not getting played--it's too much of a 'tweener for me, and I like Battle Cry and M'44 better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Nice upgrade from Memoir 44 and Battle Cry. Throws in a lot of cool things like creatures and spells and a war council but at it's heart it's still the same game. If you like the aformentioned you'll like this. For me it's all in the mood. Do I want a fantasy theme, world war II theme or civil war. It's nice to have the choice for either.

Edit: the potential for this game will never be realized. I heard Borg had rules completed for undead and elf factions, unfortunately it has been discontinued and is now out of print.

Fortunately what you do have is fully fleshed out, there are a lot of options for either the humans, dwarves or goblins. Plus it has a decent amount of creatures including a very cool hydra with remove able heads and dragons, assuming you have or can get your hands on the expansions

RATING: 2
Learning Curve: 8/10

Strategic Balance: 2/10

Player Interaction: 6/10

Thematic Direction: 7/10

Replay Addiction: 2/10

Additional Information
Why is this averaged to be 7.70? This game is a [b]poorly balanced Memoir '44 with unnecessary fluff[/b]. Although I do enjoy the assembling commander element, the overall process is quite trivial since any card is usable with enough lore. The method of obtaining lore is pure luck as well. Not to mention that 1 side has way too many archers than the other. Draw 2-3 darken the skys and the game time is shorter than the setup with 0 enjoyment.

Dwarven armies have no drawbacks and creatures are too frail. Battle back is a broken mechanic too since it makes melee combat virtually impossible.

I so wanted to enjoy this game purely for the theme and massive army control, but the [b]reliance on excessive luck makes this an expensive paperweight[/b].

RATING: 6
Yet another variation of the now famous C&C system. Components were great: nice map, pretty tiles, somewhat overdone rulebook. The minis were a bit of a letdown. They looked too similar, they were unpainted and bent, and the flag-system undermined the need for different units.

The Lore system was a nice way of countering some bad dice rolls. If you rolled a Lore symbol, you got to draw Lore tokens. It was a little hard remembering to use your cards at the right moment (particularly the Reaction cards), but it was a welcome layer to the very basic C&C game.

Overall, its just another skirmish wargame with pretty bits and streamlined rules. I found it an improvement over C&C:Ancients, but there was no wow factor for me here.
