RATING: 7
Plusses: Very clean system. VERY clean. The randomness isn't overwhelming. Cavalry works like cavalry, and heavy infantry works like heavy infantry. Very high production values: pretty pieces, nice board. I can see this game working really well with expansions.  Minuses: For some reason I felt a little blah about it after the smoke had cleared. My goblinses stomped the dwarves without too much trouble, but I think maybe I just like a little more resource management or economics or SOMETHING to hold my interest. We did play this without the lore system. There's not much of a sense of context, but that's not why anyone would play this anyway. It's not a bad game, but I truly don't get why it's rated so high.

Take 2

I've played maybe twenty or thirty more wargames between my first game of this and my second; finally I get it. (Although the level of similarity with C&C:A takes some of the shine off.) It definitely needs the lore system, and I think playing without the fancypants special powers was what made it so dull last time, although the lore cards can sometimes seem impossible to plan for. Played this with four; the Reluctant Alliance system doesn't work because there's just not enough incentive to play your own units instead of your ally's, and the cascading inefficiency that results tends to stall the game. Sure, you might say, but then some enterprising player will just sally forth with her own pieces. Minus the full cooperation of their ally, though, that tends not to work very well. The four-player game works better as a straight-up two-on-two brawl. 

The game also needs some kind of house rule to make it so you can't use lore cards to zap people who're already up against your opponent's back wall. Killing units by backing them into the backfield over and over is legit, but when you're using a magic power to do it? One that can hit anyone on the board? CHEAP.

I'd suggest: to be able to use a Lore card on a unit, any one of the following have to be true:

1) The unit is more than three spaces from the backfield.
2) A friendly unit is within three hexes of the unit.
3) The unit has attacked a friendly unit within the last round.

FFG (owner) is trying to kill Battlelore; they bought the system to take-out competition for Battles of Westeros. They published some expansions but the newer the less value you get for your money. And the expansions are overly expensive!

RATING: 7
One of those rare cases where the joy of playing the game overcomes its flaws as a simulation.  Short games; little down-time between turns; simple but agonizing decisions; decent depth for a game so abstract; and it looks great with all those miniatures.  I could just play it again and again.  The design is also amazingly elegant and clean.  The design would get this game an 8 or 9 were it not for production shortcomings:  the blue and green dice colors that look the same; the 1/2 of my miniatures that were warped and bent and required dunking and reforming in scalding water; the symbols wearing off my dice after two games.  That is strictly amateur hour and is unacceptable coming from a major game company.  Also randomness in the card draw plays a greater role in deciding the winner than I feel it should.  A powerful spell or tactical card can simply hand you the game, negating an entire game of careful maneuvering and strategizing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[2006.Dec.14] By combining the tried-and-true "Commands & Colors" (C&C) game system with a medieval/fantasy theme, Richard Borg has delivered a game that hits all the right chords. The addition of morale, support, lore (and its magical effects), war councils, as well as fantastic creatures only enriches this--the 4th--C&C title even more. Like its predecessors (i.e., Memoir '44, et al.), BattleLore's adventures (as the individual game scenarios are called) are relatively quick to play. Based on their modus operandi, only Days of Wonder could have published such a gorgeously overproduced game as this. With so many well-sculpted miniatures included in the base game, the BattleLore box is actually surprisingly quite hefty. I look forward to many, many years of enjoyable gaming from this title as well as its inevitable expansions.

[2014.Jun.14] Fun game. No doubt about it. The biggest drawback: set-up and clean-up. Still want to play though.

RATING: 6
I pretty much avoided this one like the plauge because I was afraid I was going to love it too much to say "no" to all of the expansions, and end up spending hundreds of dollars collecting a set. Well, after finally playing a friend's copy of the game I find that the game simply has too many problems for me to enjoy. The battle dice are not nearly what they should be, and there are WAY too many retreats rolled. I have never seen a battle have so many troops just running away from the action. A few retreats I can understand, but not this. The units also seem very generic, and not very specific. As many units as there are in this game I wanted to see more variances in how they operate on the board. Sadly only a few things actually make them feel different from one another.

All in all the game has a lot of good things going for it (map building, multitude of units, good card driven tactics, etc., but there are just too many things that make this game not something all too enjoyable for me.

RATING: 8
Played for the first time. This is my first try at any C&C games. And this was awesome. We played 2 times the Agincourt battle. The French won on both times. Their cavalery were murderous! The game is incredible:

- Components are some of the best in the industry and there is plenty of it. 14 differents sculpts, 3 creatures (I got it by preorder), a rulebook clear and full of examples and good illustrations.

- Gameplay is simple to learn, yet there are a lot of tough decisions.

- I like the way the scenario are made. Each scenario introduce some new concepts up to the Lore cards. There is tremendous replay value in the box.

All in all of of the best game I've played in years. Can't wait to play all the other scenarios.

UPDATE: 
Played first scenarios with Lore. This adds a lot to the game. In term of chaos and in term of pure fun. I still think that victory condition are strange. Game seem to be over before it has begun. It becomes an exercize in keeping weak unit alive. Still a very good game. Just need to get in my head: you do not win if you wipe opponent, you win if you snipe some key opponents unit.

Update: Battlelore is now a regular that I plahy once a week. We play with a custom Council and this make the game really good. Use of creature (especially Hill Giant) has change the game a lot. I still think the game is over before it has begun but it is still a very good game. My fear is that since being bold is VERY important players will tend to play really defenseive so the game would drag.

Fun. Simple Command & Colors system that when you think about it, borrows successfully from many previous game systems.  Nice figures, but I have to admit, the banner/color system I found weak at first as a piece of information design... confusing and counter-intuitive in the beginning.  Will gladly play again.

UPDATE: I lowered my rating by close to a half a point after playing Command & Colors: Ancients, which uses the same basic system.  The information design in the C&C:A production is much better, and the whole game seems cleaner and to the point.

RATING: 6
Fun to play once in a while when in the mood.  Length of setup and the need to play each scenario twice due to lack of play-balanced victory conditions are flaws keeping this from being an ideal battle system.  I prefer CCA:Ancients, though even it is a bit bloated for what should remain a simple rulesset.  My biggest complaint about Battlelore is the failure to leverage the fantasy theme with lots of creatures, magic and heroes and such, even after seemingly endless expansions.

All in all, Battlelore is a near miss.  If I am looking for a game that has a somewhat lengthy setup time and isn't necessarily all that balanced (without playing it twice and switching sides), but has great bits and you get to roll a lot of dice, I'm more likely to turn to Heroscape.  Battlelore needs more cowbell.

Looks like I might be able to re-live my Warhammer Fantasy Battle days after all!

Finally got a chance to play it, but only scenario one. And still, I like it quite a bit. Yes, it is random, yes, you don't have much control, yes it is a bit of a dice fest, but the overall barriers to entry are very low for this game so a lot of fun can be had in a short amount of time. RATING: N/A
Fast play. Not sure if it will appeal to all my students.
A ridiculously complete game system. BUT not without a handful of cons. At its heart, it's a wargame.

Bottom Line: Boardgames with Scott review just doesn't seem to do it for my students. While the mechanic is excellent, this seems a little too fiddly. Maybe Memoir 44 would have a better appeal. (Although Memoir's theme is not that appealing to them.)

At the end of the day, I think Small World will scratch the itch for a similar game.

CON: Set up and takedown is a killer. 
PRO: Lore and spell stuff can be omitted.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Simple to learn, quick to play, yet offers a lot of variety, and fun.  With a good selection of scenarios that come with the game, and even more available online to download, the average gamer should be satisfied for a long time.

The tutorials in the rule book are easy to follow, and provide a good way to learn the game basics.  The scenarios become more complex, and introduce more rules with each scenario.

The game components are also worth mentioning, as they are very well made.  Even though it might be hard at times to tell them apart, the plastic pieces look great.  The artwork is also exceptional, as well as are the cards, which are the main driving force behind the game.

Unlike most war games, Battlelore uses a rule system called the "Command and Colors system" (same as Memoir '44).  Everything a player does in the game revolves around drawing of random cards, including moving the units themselves.  While Battlelore does contain a lot of random elements, such as drawing of cards, and rolling of dice, it still involves some strategy, and tactics.

Originally, I was put off by this system, as I like to have total control over my units, but after actually playing a few games, I grew to like it.  It is simple, and fast.  Also, with usually at least 5 cards on hand to chose from, there are some tactical decisions to be made.  Unlike many other wargames, you can fight an entire battle usually in under an hour.

There are also many "cool" features the game has to offer, such as magic, customizable "Lore Masters" (which bring quite a bit of strategy to the table), and monsters.  Unfortunately, there is only one monster in the base game, and the other two are hard to get, as they were limited time only offers.  

However, since Fantasy Flight Games took over the Battlelore license, thing are looking up.  There are already two expansions on the horizon, and possibly more in the future that might offer more new monsters!  

Of course, there is already a handful of expansions out there, that make the game more interesting; especially the Call To Arms expansion, and A Hundred Years' War.  

All in all, this is a fun little game that is easy to learn, and will offer hours, and hours of fun.

RATING: 8
Thanks to Scharri7 for this game, which I scored in the BGG.CON 2009 math trade. I even acquired a second copy, but since getting it I've only gotten it back to the table once. Which makes me sad.

I like the game quite a bit, as it hits a good spot along the length/depth/complexity axes. I think the cards--and the attendant possibility of getting shut out where you want to move--work well in a light game like this. Not so well, I expect, in a heavier game.

Lore is a nice addition, as it adds not only magic but fun swings of fortune. The lore cards are interesting and fun and seem to add just the right amount of unpredictability. And of course the monsters are cool.

I do wish the set-up/tear-down time was less. This one is best if you have a dedicated opponent and want to play several games in a row.

I like that this is a little more complex and a little bit meatier than Memoir '44. I'd go with Memoir for a quicker game, I'd go with this for something with a similar feel but more complex. Both are handy to have, as they fill satisfying niches. If I could somehow carry around 20 games at all times, just in case a game opportunity came up, both of these would be among them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
This two-player game uses the same basic mechanics as Memoir '44: miniature units on hexes are moved by "command cards" that state which sub-class of units can move and/or attack.  This game adds more complexity (and opportunities for strategy) when compared with Memoir '44: units have morale effects, unit attack values are dependent on both the type of unit and the color of flag (so unit strength varies widely), and there are now a separate set of Lore cards.  The lore cards are an excellent addition, as they add more tactical and strategic possibilities to the game, and require careful planning regarding when and how to use them.  The addition of morale is another great change, as it makes unit placement much more strategic (e.g., units with high morale can counterattack when attacked). While there is much opportunity for tactical and strategic thinking, at its core this game is still heavily luck based (all attacks are based on die rolls, and what units you can command depends entirely on the cards in front of you).  If you're looking for a straight up wargame, this isn't for you. 

Update (March 2007): I just played an Epic scenario with four players, and it was great; being able to play two command cards and work with another player was great.

Update (June 2007): I just played Call to Arms, and that expansion makes the game even better. It's great to be able to choose (to some extent) where the troops go.

Update (January 2009): The wisdom of time, and having now played another unit-based combat game (Conflict of Heroes: AtB), has led me to realize that Battlelore isn't all that I thought it was.  The use of cards to drive unit movement is highly limiting tactically, the setup time is far too long, and the unbalanced scenarios are just frustrating.  Due to these problems it turns out that I haven't played this game in more than a year and a half (not consciously ... we just stopped suggesting it).  Thus I'm lowering my rating from the original 8.5 to 7.75.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.75
It's very clear the publisher went the extra mile to make the bits high-quality; the rule book is written for the entry-level gamer, thwarting frustration by easing newbies into the increasingly-complex and elaborate rules system.  BattleLore is a contemporary wargame with a modular ruleset and dynamic play through the lore/war council scenarios. This makes the game better than its companion title, Battle Cry.   Some shortfalls from the aspect of this old-school wargamer:  the board seemed a bit cramped, possibly by design to hasten  gameplay by expediting conflict.  Also, for the experienced gamer - forget the basic game.  Without the lore system, this is a medicore wargame with pretty bits. Another thing that could detract from the fun are the random elements: (dice), and the command cards vary sufficiently in power to create an imbalanced game between similarly skilled players from time to time.  Overall, though - the game is sufficienly polished to minimize this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Richard Borg created a niche for himself with the Commands and Colors series. Since Memoir, I've spent a lot of time in that niche - especially with BattleLore.

Add in Call to Arms, Epic, War councils, creatures, and now new specialist units. Don't bother buying this game unless you want to buy into the system. 

Days of Wonder planned ahead on this game, and it shows. The expansions plug in like missing pieces to a big puzzle. You can play BattleLore without Call to Arms or Epic, but when you add them in you start realizing that the base game is incomplete. Some may not go for this, thinking it too much of a collectible game. I 'm all for it. Sometimes I want to play a miniatures game without all the hassle, and it's just easier to play BattleLore.

Playing pre-generated scenarios is fun, but you only get so much out of them. With the expansions that have been released thus far, the whole system moves into the realm of army customization without adding too much time to the game's setup. Pick a deployment deck. Pick a few specialist cards. Assign your war council. Assign your deployment cards and place your reserve units, and start playing. It's efficient, yet gives you endless combinations of war council and specialist cards.

Setting up the units remains the worst part of the Commands and Colors games. BattleLore is no different, and adds in banner bearers, so it takes longer than Memoir or C&C: Ancients.

The most impressive part is the rulebook - not the full color perfect bound-ness of it, but the clarity.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I purchase Battlelore based upon the good press, and while some of this I now believe to be more hype than substance, Battlelore is enjoyable.  I don't like it as much as others, but I do like the blend of historical and fantasy, and I like the customizable aspect of the War Council.  There is a lot of room for both strategy and tactics, and I like the additions that new units and rules will provide.  The game is also produced wonderfully; playing card sized references for specific units/weapons/terrain is a really nice addition.

My concern is that much of the strategy can be undone by very powerful lore cards.  Because the lore accumulates endlessly, casting a huge spell is almost only a matter of drawing it and waiting.  I think what ultimately saves Battlelore is the short playtime.  You can play several games in one session, and this alleviates somewhat the possibility of big swings due to lore.  I enjoy the game and the possibilities it presents, but ultimately its a little too light and chaotic for me to devote lots of time to it.

RATING: 7
Battlelore 1st edition is fun and offers a lot of game components and options.  A ton really.  The game can be surprisingly simple or really complex.  Both ways to play are fun.  A lot of fun depending on who you are.

There are a lot of complaints about luck being involved.  I'll say from the BASIC PLAY stand point, there is a good amount of luck, not a bad amount.  The following is a review of basic play mechanics.

There are two main components of luck.  COMMAND CARDS and the BATTLE DICE.  COMMAND CARDS decide what sort of actions you can take on your turn, you start off with a fixed amount depending on the scenario and you draw one at the end of your turn.  COMMAND CARDS say things such as: Move up to 3 units on the left side of the map.  Or Move up to as many Green banner units as to how many command cards you have including this one.  They are flexible and you'll always have a few to choose from.  There is a fun bit of meta gaming involved as you have to decide which command to play and think a turn or two ahead so you can combo up movements strategically.  Consider COMMAND CARDS thematically, they represent what you're in position to command.  As an overlord in charge of an army, you're rarely in a situation/position where you can aptly tell everyone what to do.

BATTLE DICE are just fun luck factors.  There is a 1/3 to 1/2 chance to affect the battle field depending on your attacking unit and position.  There is a chance to cause damage and/or cause a break in the enemy's morale and force them to retreat.  The retreat/pursuit mechanics are a ton of fun and can really intensify the state of battle.

The order of things makes a lot of sense and if you do the historical campaigns they can really make you feel like you're commanding the souls of your troops.  You can become inspired to develop your own campaign and house rules.

Battlelore is a fun wargame-lite.  I personally don't own any wargames as of writing this, so maybe I'm ignorant to what the genre has to offer, but I sincerely want to play this every night at least once.  It's quick and easy and is as immersive as you and your opponent let it be.  [I go for maximum immersion].

RATING: 9

RATING: 7
Battlelore takes the Command & Colours game engine and turns it into its most flexible and expandable form yet.  This game has the rules and promise that make me think that in a year's time, my opinion of 'What is Battlelore?' might be totally different, since upcoming expandions might offer a completely different game experience.  For now, it's a fantastic combat game with lots of variety and lots of options.  The lore deck really adds some flavour to the game, and ensures that identical scenarios will almost never play out the same way.

June 2008: Knocked a point off the rating because I don't like the way DoW has gone about expanding the game.  Where did the cheap blister packs and cool monsters go?  Starting to feel too much like 40k with set armies and less like Magic with infinite interchangeability.

February 2009: Knocked another point off. The franchise has lost its way and I will play on occasion but this hasn't lived up to my hopes.

RATING: 8
I love the C&C game system.  The scenarios are short enough that you can play 3 or 4 in a single sitting.  Teaching the game to new players is relatively easy due to the incremental adding of rules, and if there's a part of the rules that's too complex for them, you can just play a scenario that doesn't include those rules.  Your strategy matters (I have opponents who don't have a good grasp of the strategy, and they lose probably 8 out of 10 games), but the randomness of the cards and dice gives anyone a chance to win on any given game (which is important for getting people to play again).  It's a very pretty game, and it's just plain fun to play with.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
So I broke down and bought BattleLore, after I told myself that I didn't need it.  Turns out, 'past self' was right - to a point.

As a preamble, I own Memoir 44, BattleLore and Command and Colours: Ancients.  They are all created by Richard Borg and all use a similar system.  Each have their own merits and I'll say this:  If you have either of the other games, reach for BattleLore only if you want to try the same dice and card system but with a fantasy theme.

If Command and Colours: Ancients were to be considered the 'advanced' of the Borg system of combat, then BattleLore would be considered 'intermediate' - M'44, basic.  

BattleLore takes the presentation of M'44, the raw concept of C&C:A, and jams it into a pseudo-fantasy box - clearly with the intent on making an independent game 'system' that no longer requires specific scenarios.  It does away with the intricacies of C&C:A, yet empowers the player more than with M'44.  The Lore system is truly why people come to play, and I look forward to seeing how I can further enjoy and exploit that system.

Where BattleLore truly succeeds is in pure presentation.  An expensive game, yes, but the lavish production of all of the components (packaging included) is the best I've ever seen.  The rulebook is beautiful, thorough, well designed.  The miniatures are a mini-painter's dreamland. Everything is very good.  Without reservation, this game is a solid 8, but if you have been reared on the complexity and control of C&C:A, only reach for this game if you'd like to take that basic system and add Goblins.  And Dwarves.  And giant spiders.

RATING: 9
From top to bottom, this is a beautiful, well-executed work that delivers everything I had hoped it would.

The packaging is gorgeous, no surprise since it was originally produced by Days of Wonder. I know folks have problems with the bendy miniatures...I have many myself...but in the scheme of things, to have a couple of hundred individual characters such as these, well, I think it's worth a few imperfections. Everything was already separated upon opening the box, which I love as well. The board is nice...quite like Memoir '44, obviously...it could even be bigger, I think, but it functions just fine and is set up to add additional boards for Epic Battlelore. Nice.

The manual is an example of how game manuals should be produced. Clear prose with many illustrations make it a pleasure to learn. The scenarios are set up so that each one builds upon the last and generally adds another level of play, making the learning curve fun and simple to grasp. The C&C system works wonderfully here, and we absolutely love the addition of Lore and the war council. 

Setup takes a while, but I think this is a function of finding a better way to store the pieces compartmentally. I truly hope that FFG makes available the extra creatures because it's a shame to think we won't be able to acquire them for a reasonable price, which might make them prohibitive.  

I could play this game all day.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Splendid game here.  This is a little more complex than Memoir '44, while preserving the beauty of the visuals that is missing from C&C Ancients.  I like the lore system a lot, and the use of terrain for simple effects without bogging the player down in a gazillion modifiers is a nice touch.  The bravery system works well to keep players putting their units near each other, which then serves to create concerns about area of effect lore powers and mobility concerns.  I like it a lot.

RATING: 9
I love this game: it fulfils my desire for miniatures gaming and wargaming in one go, and with excellent components and a short playing time. What more can I ask for? I rate this game so highly that I will be selling all of my (still mostly unpainted) miniatures. Why would I want to spend months painting figures and building battlefields in order to play a miniatures game that lasts all afternoon when I can just set up Battlelore and play 3 games in 3 hours?

RATING: 7.5
(1/15/08) - Just got this today. I look forward to a nice evolution in the C&C System.

(1/21/08) - I decided to paint all of the miniatures in the game. A significant undertaking, but it will make the game look 10x better!

(7/19/08) - [7.5] - With 3 plays of the basic game under my belt I am ready to give BattleLore a rating. SOLD (04/29/2017) - Battlelore has been sold. It just never got to the table very much. Unfortunately my custom paint job for the miniatures made them quite sticky to handle, which did not provide much incentive to play.

RATING: 8
Even a decade after its release I still have a lot of admiration for this game. The bold attempt to make a 1-box board game that covers all medieval battles plus fantasy elements and do it without a complex rule set. Hundreds of scenarios are available online which offers great replay options. This still the most family-friendly war game I've ever encountered and deserves respect for that alone. Offers a history lesson with some fun fantasy thrown in. We used the Battles of Westeros figures to add Elvish and Viking factions which add some welcome variety to the armies as well.

RATING: 4
Things I liked about BattleLore - The ranged combat seen in Mem '44 has been curtailed resulting in more melee (adjacent) combat.  In addition, a taste of the the complexity of units from C&C:Ancients has been carried into this system.  System can be used to simulate historical battles or (via the addition of lore) fantasy battles with creatures and special squads (dwarves / goblins).  Works well to force strategic use of line combat and keeping units together for good effect. 

But in the end still a fantasy game with fantastic units.  Would much rather play C&C:A which isn't all that much more difficult to play and doesn't have the magic which can be a bit too powerful.  Prefer the historic battles, but the product line seems more focused on the fantasy/magic.  Traded my copy away to focus instead of C&C:A and its expansions instead.

RATING: 9
After I bought Battles of Westeros and this game became (even) harder to find, I bit the bullet and bought the copy that had been staring at me from my FLGS's shelves.
I haven't played this nearly enough, but I'm glad I own it.
Components are beautiful! With a different miniature for each unit type and cups to store your lore tokens, this game might even be called 'overproduced'. 
The tutorial-like buildup of the scenario's included in this box make it necessary to buy at least the 'Call to Arms'expansion, so you can play with randomly deployed forces and the full ruleset that the scenario build-up took so much effort to teach you.

Currently going through the 'tutorial-scenario's' with Moonhawk (using his copy), 9 down - 1 to go. After that, we plan on playing his copy of the Scottish Wars expansion. We've dipped our toe in a Call to Arms game as well.

[b][u]Painted the miniatures?:[/u][/b] No and I probably won't, as it would be a very monumental task, including the expansions... [b]EDIT:[/b] I got a cheap second copy, just to paint up. I'm making progress on painting.

RATING: 4.5
A game I've not yet been able to master (and now because I got rid of it, I never will). 

I understand the basics of the strategy (I think) but seem to always be bested in spite of anything I try. 

Possibly becuase of this the game always leaves me cold -it lacks the draw of lots of other light-warlike games.

The mechanics are interesting - but I'd claim that, like most 'minis' games by board games the minis are completely irrelevant and the same game could be made with just counters and cards (Command and Colours...?). More so here, perhaps, as the game doesn't feel like a minis game and lacks much of the tactical positioning/line of sight/terrain features I'd assocate with Mini Games. 

I get what the game is trying to give you, but too often it seems like it's pure chance whether me or my opponent gets the opportunity to execute their plan...

The minis are of reasonable quality, but often bent and even hot/cold-water treatment has a hard time fixing them.  

Addendum: I tried, and really enjoyed command and colours, so I really don't know what the problem here was...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a great euro-style war game.  Players face off across a map with 3 sections: Left, Center, Right.  Each player gets a number of Command Cards (determined by the scenario).  On their turn they play a Command Card and then activate the troops noted on the card.  This means that you are constantly revising your tactics as you may not have the cards to command the section of the board you want (i.e. you are set for a killing blow on the Right section but none of your Command Cards activate the Right section).  There is great depth of strategy and plenty of replay value.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An interesting twist on the C&C system. The lore cards and the battle council add some customization to each side. There are also a number of expansions which add different troops with unique abilities.

RATING: 8
I though that I didn't like wargames, but this probably proves me wrong, even when I have only played Medieval battles.  Perhaps, as most of wargamers say, this isn't a real wargame, due of its simplicity and lack of reality.  I understand that players may feel frustrated about the card system:  Somebody could have managed to get a war machine ready to crush over his/her rival, and the card he/she needs doesn't show up.  Is that realistic?  Well, I believe yes.  Or is it more realistic in a battle field that only two or three units fight meanwhile everybody else watches to each other.  The card mechanic allows to put in play all of the sectors in the map.  This is a great game with an intuitive flow of play that, despite the 80 pages of manual, goes on its way very smooth.  Besides, the great replayability, thanks to lots of scenarios, and the excelent editorial work (odor of the inside of the box apart) makes this game one of the best around.  A nice addition to the series, which has titles as [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/551]Battle Cry[/url][/i], [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/10630]Memoir '44[/url][/i], [i][url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/game/14105]Command and Colors:  Ancients[/url][/i] and [i]Command and Colors:  Napoleonics[/i].

RATING: 6
I was down on the whole idea of BattleLore when I first heard about it.  Two things changed my mind: 1) They changed the sell-mode to standard expansion lines; 2) I played C&C:A and saw the effect of support rules, etc. on how the game would play.  Now I've actually played it, both with and without lore, and though I'm not convinced that lore doesn't end up overwhelming the rest of the game, I /am/ convinced that BattleLore is as different from the other C&C games as any of them are.  I wouldn't necessarily recommend it to everyone, but if you can handle having multiple C&C titles on your shelf, this one is as worthy as any.  I admit to being particularly excited about some of the expansion possibilities, especially the "design your own scenario" options in Call to Arms.  I could easily see my rating going up from that alone.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Initially picked up as a painting project, and because its 15mm figures look nice as giants/monsters next to my fledgling mass of 6mm and 10mm armies. 

It's a fine game in its own right; See my thoughts on C&C:A. Battlelore's goofy-fun aesthetics are a nice match with the lite C&C mechanic. Plus, i'm a huge sucker for miniatures, particularly games w/ masses of them. The caricature-ish sculpts and creature expansions go great with the theme. 

I am, unfortunately, unconvinced of the actual "lore" part of the game. While it adds a bit of flavor and goofy fun, its implementation feels a bit too abstract, too random, a bit too tacked-on; It detracts from the core C&C mechanic. It's like two diametrically opposed gaming goals: Managing the randomness of the C&C hand draw (which is awesome, yay) combined with the luck of a game-changing lore card (not so awesome if, like me, you enjoy C&C for its hand-management.)

Some notes: 

I'll admit, the lore part is fun if you're in goofy-fun mode -- which brings up the 2nd Edition, which I am passing on for now. The 2E's art direction, while awesome in its own right, does nothing for me C&C-wise. It seems 2E, with its darker aesthetic and 28mm figs, is shooting directly at the Warhammer player base. And I think it's a mistake to use the C&C mechanic for it. 

Seriously, though, if FFG were really going for "Warhammer-lite" they already have a great starting place at Battles of Westeros.     

Speaking of BoW, I was curious to see if the liteness of Battlelore would make for a more-enjoyable playing experience than the engrossing, though more fiddly, BoW. It does not. The more miniatures and "miniatures-like" boardgames I come across, the more I'm convinced BoW, though far from perfect, is the best miniatures-like boardgame out there.

RATING: 7
I like the gameplay, but I'm a big fan of C&C:A, so that's no surprise.

The unit strengths are somewhat different between the two games - enough so that one's tactical decisions especially must be altered from what they would be in C&C:A. The production value of the game is excellent, and I really like the art work and the development of scenarios - gradually introducing new wrinkles as you go.

I do *not* like the little plastic men. They're unpainted and hence unattractive to look at - I guess I prefer the wooden block aesthetic. And no, I do not have the time to paint them. I'd rather spend that time actually playing games! Well, I probably will spray paint them, both for looks and because of a color problem. I cannot understand why game publishers continue to make life miserable for those of us who have color vision impairments. I cannot tell the difference between the red banners and the green banners in this game, and am forced to tell which is which by memory in scenarios where the ambiguity arises. I figure spray painting the figures in different shades of red/green will allow me to tell the difference at a glance.

I also find the separate information cards irritating, and would prefer a single sheet which summarizes the different kinds of units and terrain. Throughout, Days of Wonder opted for flash instead of practicality when it came to the materials in this game. Take the dice: why couldn't they have used different symbols for light, medium, and heavy units? Would that have been so difficult? As it is, I have to have my opponent tell me whether the symbols I rolled are red or green. Grrrr! Also, on the summary card, mounted units are called "Long Sword" units. Elsewhere, they are called "Mounted". A little consistency would have been nice. All these little details, despite the lavish production, are really irritating as they create confusions which hamper actually *playing* the game. If someone were to express interest in the Commands and Colors system, I would not hesitate to recommend that they pick up Ancients instead of BattleLore if only for the reason that the production of Ancients is streamlined in a way that makes it easy to learn and play the game. Usefulness trumps glitz.

Other than that, it's a good game. Familiarity with Ancients allowed me to get over the irritations here, but in the end I prefer Ancients. BattleLore emphasizes chance over skill as compared to Ancients, and I don't care for this. Confronted with both games, I will always choose to play Ancients.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Okay - first thing I want to say is that this game is really a luck fest. You may think that all the luck can be ameliorated because of the amount of dice rolls there is in a game, and with tactics and strategy, ...but come on! Luck to the rolls, luck to the draw of the cards, etc. Im just sayin...

But! Having said that, and now saying I generally don't like high luck factor in games, I will say this:

I love it!!! 

Really, it is so much fun!! Just pure fun! Love the magic system, and the bits, and the beautiful colors, and the terrain, and the creatures, blah blah. I mean, if you go in knowing that there is a high degree of luck, and then just let go and start your maneuvering and planning and attacking and defending, and being bold and archering, etc, you will find there are tons of decisions and tactical concerns and delicious moments of high tension each game. Highly recommended.

RATING: 8
Simple but elegant mechanics that make play move quickly.  Definitely enjoyable to play.  My only complaint, and this isn't necessarily a bad thing for everyone, is the diciness-- combat is very swingy with only a few dice being rolled at a time, and battlebacks possibly causing your swarming attack to be devastated.  Your choices are also constricted by what command cards you draw (arrrgh, all my cards activate my empty left flank!) and what lore cards you have.  Obviously, working around these limitations and hedging your bets is a huge part of the strategy, but it may turn off gamers who are looking for more skill-oriented games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I'm totally into the Battle, not so much into the Lore.  My wife and I have played "Battle Cry" for years, but I've only played the base game of "Memoir '44" a couple of times and I've never tried "Commands and Colors: Ancients".  Instead, we leaped right up in the line to "BattleLore".  So my obvious comparison is going to be "Battle Cry".  At first, I was unsure what to think of "BattleLore", since it is ultimately a very similar game to "Battle Cry" (and not just in title!).  My initial impression was that all the newly added elements in the game simply created unnecessary complication and fiddliness, since they ultimately didn't do much to change the luck factor or the same, dull winning conditions of grabbing flags.  And I also felt that all the fantasy elements really ruined what could have been a great, historical game.  But then I realized that the game is entirely customizable, and all I had to do was pick up the "Call to Arms" and the "Hundred Years War" expansions, utilize the "medieval lore" rules, leave out the war council, goblins, dwarves, etc., and I could have a perfectly playable historical game.  I also realized that they have improved a number of other things from the "Battle Cry" days, from the way in which weapons are utilized to the command cards themselves.  And the production value is stunning (as has come to be expected with Days of Wonder)!  Sure, the amount of packaging is sickening (especially with the expansions), the cartoonish artwork is nothing short of grotesque (they could have done SO much better with a little bit of realism or, at the very least, with a more "Mr. Jack" or "Jamaica"-style of cartoon pleasantry), the set-up time is excruciating (even preventing the game from getting to the table at times), and the level of strategy can feel both very limiting and ahistorical at times.  But the attention to detail with everything in the game right down to the rulebook and all those reference cards makes the game exceptionally easy to pick up and play.  (The makers of "War of the Ring" need to talk to whomever it was that created the "BattleLore" rulebook and reference cards).  This is a fantastic game!  I have even traded away my beloved "Battle Cry" now that I own this one.  If only it wasn't such a huge pain to set up!  Game design: 4.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 4/5  Total: 8.5/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I don't understand why this game is consistently considered the "best" of Richard Borg's "Command and Colors" line of games. I like this game, but I don't think the added complexity (specifically the Lore cards) add any tactical depth. When it comes down to it, these games are largely luck-driven -- one can try to "strategerize" for about three turns, given the cards in one's hand. Adding more cards just gives you more to consider, but in my opinion, doesn't really change the gameplay in any meaningful way. In fact, in my opinion, it detracts from the game. 

I certainly appreciate the excellent production value, but gameplay is more important to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This... is... AWESOME!  First and for most I want to say how impressed I am with this title, DOW really is in my opinion one of the best game companies of all time.  Everything about Battlelore is over produced, gorgeous and delightful.  I find myself longing for the expansion releases and can’t wait until they release more armies (undead in particular if they ever do).  The game itself holds great variety without the addon components of the future and can only leave you excited by the new possibilities.  In my opinion the entirety of battlelore and its potential makes it a FAR superior game to say Warhammer Fantasy.  The 70 dollar price tag for 2 armies, a board, beautiful components and minis, its unbelievable compared to anyone who has tried to play a Games workshop game.  I’m impressed by DOW that much is clear.  

While it does take a little time to set up (More then Memoir’ 44) it’s completely worth it.  That being said I have played only the first three scenarios so far.  The idea that these scenarios range from historical medieval to fantasy is wonderful and just shows that the game is meant to be completely versatile.   The first two were heavy dice related without to much strategy (basically pure Memoir’ 44 style games) the third however was far more interesting.  The third scenario brings Moral rules to the table and introduces the goblinoids (with an interesting skirmish dynamic all their own).  Needless to say after this battle I was SOLD on Battlelore.  I cannot WAIT to play Scenario 5 which introduces lore to the game and sounds incredibly unique.  

The Lore rules create an interesting dynamic pertaining to your physical combat misses translates into powerful spells.  I adore the concept and it seems to elevate the entire systems random elements.  Lore definitely seems to give more decisions to the game and creates a brilliant dynamic I cannot WAIT to try (you can tell by the rules alone.)  The monstrous creatures excite me as well, I really cannot wait to see what else they come out with.  That being said the 80 page rulebook is solidly written and is detailed, laid out in a completely perfect way.  The scenarios build and build making you want more, watching the games rules unfold as you play.  The reference card system is welcome as it was in Memoir’ 44 but I find myself wishing they just had one larger card vs a bunch of small ones, however we are lucky they provide these convenient summaries in the first place.  While the production is fantastic I find the packing method to be somewhat unfortunate.  The plastic bubble boxes crush the armies resulting in bent figures (I rather like the soft plastic though, rather bent figures than broken ones).  DOWs website support also suggests great things to come.  They have provided the game with more scenarios and even a creator.  I cannot wait to see how this evolves, I suspect that further plays will result in a 9 from me.

Addendum: Done the book, even better.

RATING: 6
Miniatures are superfluous, difficult to identify and fussy to manipulate; would play the same, but setup would be faster and the game cheaper with blocks, by which I mean blocks with 1 step per side, rather than C&C:Ancients wasteful block system.

Randomness is too high in C&C to begin with; Lore takes any strategy in the game and chucks it out the window. Scenarios are unbalanced. "Play it twice, once on each side." is not a balancing mechanism; it's lazy game development.

The fantasy element is superfluous. Dwarves = elite units, goblins = skirmish units. Strict historical would have been preferred, but I suppose it would have appealed less to those who don't grok history.

RATING: 6
For my general opinion on this 'series' of games by Richard Borg, see my comments on "Battle Cry".

This game, together with Memoir '44, strikes me as the best of the bunch.  With inclusion of different characters and the lore subsystem, and its many expansions, this strikes me as potentially the deepest of this 'series'.  It also therefore likely has the greatest replay value, but is also certainly the most time-consuming (never a point in a game's favour, and to my mind, doubly a drawback here, since even with the additional complications, there's just not that much depth).

Update: On reflection, I was probably too hard on these games with my initial ratings, so I've upped them a bit.  Even my initial rating and comments weren't meant to indicate that I didn't like them or wouldn't play them - I do enjoy them, and will happily play them from time to time - but none of them really grab me.

RATING: 8
Fun and quite light game. Very random with all the card draws and dice rolls, but in a really fun way. There are some meaningful decisions to make in order to maximize your chances for success. You want to roll many dice to let the mana flow, avoid battle backs, keep your units in support, move your troops to the areas for which you have good cards, use the terrain to your advantage and make good use of your mana. Before the game, you need to balance your ability to use different types of spells versus the power of spells of a certain type and the draws for commands and spells.

RATING: 8.5
A game I truly like, but due to the fickleness of the dice and the random nature of the system this game doesn't hit my table often. 

The game's biggest strength, is also it's biggest flaw: you receive cards, and these determine which orders you can give your armies. This simplifies the game and speeds it up nicely, but it can also cripple your chances of winning. You'll just have to make the best of your cards, and if that means your top infantry stays useless on the left flank because you only have cards to activate your wimpy archers on the right flank, that's too bad. But, your opponent has the same problem. This does prevent analysis paralysis, since you basically have to pick the best choice you have availabloe out of 5 or so options.

Also, a nice catch is the way that some misses in combat give you more 'mana' to play magic spells. This is a smart way to boost players that have rotten luck with the dice.

All in all, a good (and simple) game that can be played relatively quick, but be aware that the randomness of the cards and dice means you better not expect a grand strategy to win, but a healthy dose of luck and sound tactics.

RATING: 8
Finally rated this after 3 plays. I haven't gotten in any Lore Scenarios yet, so I'm not familiar with the whole system. However, even without them this is a solid 8. With more plays, it could definitely go up. Unfortunately, it's not really my wife's thing. She'll play it with me (for which I'm eternally grateful!), but she's not exactly a direct conflict kind of gal.

Anyway, the system is very elegant. The command cards you receive are random and the colors (and other stuff) you roll are random, but it's still extremely strategic and tactical. I'm impressed with how Borg is able to incorporate numerous special units, terrains, and effects with only subtle changes to the basic rules.

Lastly, despite the large rulebook (80+ pages IIRC), it is fairly simple system. In fact, they include rule cards (playing card size) which can be dealt out to each player. Basically ALL of the rules are on these cards. And they're not super text-heavy either. For the first scenario, Agincourt, all the necessary rules are on 9 cards. That's a cleaner ruleset that most Euros.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
BattleLore arrives finally and finally I've had a chance to play it.  Seems to be a very nice refinement of the rules and system to date.  There's a lot more going on in this than in say Memoir or Battlecry. That takes away from this being as much of a 'gateway' game and more of a gamers game. I found myself having to make more decsions and they were harder to choose from.  Some of the cards in the command deck from the previous games have migrated over to the 'Lore' deck now so to use them you have spend lore.  Lore system works fine if you ask me and it's really nice to have lore side of the dice earn you lore instead of doing nothing at all like in previous games.  I do think that if you are going to have a system like this however it should have been a little more in depth.  It's good, but not great.

Now to the crux of it for me.  I love this game and will be playing a lot of it.  I'm gonna go ahead and say that I like M'44 better still.  It's faster and easier and I can teach that game to non-gamers and convert them instantly.  Theme wise I enjoy WWII better than fantasy as well, but that's not going to stop me from playing BattleLore, just a statement. The C&C system is brilliant and another excellent product is added here.

UPDATE. My initial thoughts do seem to be on par and for the most part accurate. I have played a LOT of BattleLore as predicted but that also means I've kind of burned myself out on it. I don't think I'll be playing this nearly as much anymore and interestingly enough M'44 still hits the table just about as much as it ever did.

RATING: 5
The third game in the C&C series that I tried (having previously played Memoir '44 and Commands & Colors: Ancients), and the third disappointment.  It's not a bad game, but I fail to see what's so great about this series of games.  I have a few complaints with this series: 1) the setup time is ridiculous, it takes forever to get all the terrain and units in place; 2) the amount of luck is unbelievable and the dice just never go my way... ever; 3) the games are not especially complex but the rules are very fiddly, they're not at all streamlined because there are tons of little things to remember like all the modifiers for the dice; 4) I don't usually care much about theme, but I don't see why you'd bother making games about WWII, ancient warfare, and elves/dwarves if they're not going to change much, so it never actually feels different regardless of what theme is pasted on.  This is not to say that these are the worst games ever, but they're just not especially great.  They're alright and I'd be willing to play again, but won't be actively looking for another game.

See my Review: http://games.fooville.net/nycgamer-article-candcgamefactory.html

RATING: 8
An excellent game that combines some of the better aspects of Wargaming with cards.  Beautifully presented with quality miniatures, board, counters, and one of the better Rule Books I've seen.  

They did a wonderful job with the learning curve on this one.  Each scenario adds new units and concepts, until you're up to playing with Lore, monsters, defensive positions, etc.

Setup time is a bit of a bear... you'll probably want to buy some kind of small box to keep the minis straight if you get a copy.

DoW also has an excellent website dedicated to this game, complete with a scenario editor and user created scenarios.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I come from a table top background and my wife isnt into all the rules of table top games.  So we gave this game a try and both love it.  It uses colored dice instead of your standard D6 and movement is done using a deck of cards that you draw and use.  Different colored banners show the different types of troops.  For instance, there are green, blue, and red colored banners in which green is "easy" blue is "medium" and red is "hard/elite" what I mean by that is red color normally gets 4 attacks (4 dice) and green 1-2 attacks (1-2 dice).  How the game is set up is you pick out a scenario in the adventure book that comes with the game.  So your troop types and numbers are already worked out for you.  You do not make a troop list (although you can create your own adventures as well!)  

There is another "advanced" mechanic that allows you to use different cards that give "buff/spells" called lore.  These are used to create more of a fantasy feel to the game.

Why I rate this game a 10:
1.It has a historic play. i.e. recreating famous battles that really happened.
2.It can also have a fantasy play to it as well.  Goblins and Dwarfs come with the game as well as normally men. And the lore mechanic adds to this feel as well.
3.Its a table top game on a game board.  easy to play, easy to TEACH, fast games, like scrabble in that fact that you only have the movement cards in front of you to use.  Like the letters for scrabble.
4.able to paint the models if you WANT to. Which we are.  Also the models are about 15mm. FYI Games Workshop (warhammer fantasy and warhammer 40,000) are about 35mm heroic scale.

RATING: 6
a game I just want to love and be obsessed with. but the set up and the mechanics just are not matched by the actual gameplay.

I don't mind the randomness of the dice...that seems standard and are mitigated with interesting card play or good tactics.

I do mind the cards themselves, because here all your decision making is reduced to a few random cards with way too much variability. It is frustrating to not be able to do what you need to do because you lack the proper flank. Also, some cards are so overpowered, and some lore strategies are so overpowered that the luck is even worse.

I still plan on playing it whenever I can manage it, but I won't be drooling over it, or over the expansions and such.

For what is in essence a non-collectible miniature game on a board (I did not play any C&C games before), the rules are quick to explain thanks to some great reference cards, downtime is perfectly acceptable and setup time is bearable. The game has great components. What I especially like is its value for money: The game can be easily adapted to your mood, as you can play anything from a skirmish between generic troops to a full-blown war with creatures, different terrain, characters and magic. Great stuff.
UPDATE: I like the game better and better with more plays and more options. As soon as you have added Lore and the War Council to your games, you will never go back. Not sure about the giant creatures though - they add quite a bit of fiddly rules. Always wanted to play your miniature tabletop games with nongamers? With Battlelore you can! :-). Played 8 times sofar.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
The world of BattleLore meshes history and fantasy together - putting players in command of an array of miniature troops on the battlefields of a Medieval Europe Uchronia at the outset of the Hundred Years War.

Drawing on the strengths of Memoir '44, Days of Wonder's newest game takes the time-tested Command and Colors system to a new level and offers gamers of many backgrounds a chance to fight medieval battles with a dose of epic fantasy.

In this fantastical re-imagining of the Hundred Years War, French and English armies are supplemented with Goblins and Dwarves mercenaries and even some creatures like Giant Spider and Earth Elemental! Just as important as the armies you have, though, are the Lore Masters you choose to aid you: Wizards, Clerics, Warriors and Rogues can all aid you with unique powers and spells in ways role-playing gamers will find familiar.

RATING: N/A
This game is based upon Richard Borg's Command and Colors system. The world of BattleLore meshes history and fantasy together - putting players in command of an array of miniature troops on the battlefields of a Medieval Europe Uchronia at the outset of the Hundred Years War.
\13\\13\
Drawing on the strengths of Memoir '44, this Days of Wonder game takes the time-tested Command and Colors system to a new level and offers gamers of many backgrounds a chance to fight medieval battles with a dose of epic fantasy.
\13\\13\
In this fantastical re-imagining of the Hundred Years War, French and English armies are supplemented with Goblins and Dwarves mercenaries and even some creatures like the Giant Spider and the Earth Elemental! Just as important as the armies you have, though, are the Lore Masters you choose to aid you: Wizards, Clerics, Warriors and Rogues can all aid you with unique powers and spells in ways role-playing gamers will find familiar.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Resource management and miniatures with a fantasy war game theme.

This game has a simple war game theme much like Commands and Colors: Ancients, Memoir '44 and Battle Cry, but with the added twist that you collect lore and pay lore to cast spells from lore cards that are obtained along the way.  You'd think this would swing the game wildly, but both players have the same opportunities to cast devastating (but not game-breaking) spells along the way.

The game is totally overproduced with more bits than you really need to play the game, but the bits make the game more fun with a great deal of visual appeal.  Plus, all the complex rules are on handy tip cards, so I don't have to decode a table (like in C&C:A) to figure out what I can do, and what the response is. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Normal Adventures: 19
Epic Adventures: 7
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Like most of the Command and Colors games, the mechanics of BattleLore are a mixed bag. The historical rules have the right feel, as battles tend to break down into small clusters of engaged units, the way I imagine that encounters of that era tended to play out. The command card system does a good job of simulating communication difficulties on the Medieval battlefield (sometimes you need to order a unit and just can't get the message through) and the combat dice make skirmish resolution short and sweet.

The lore rules on the other hand, which add fantasy creatures and spell casters are not nearly as well designed (especially the spell cards) and in fact seem to be hugely overpowered. Almost every spell seems under costed by half, resulting in very swingy game play. Some cards can effectively kill three or four units with one order. Considering most scenarios end at six or seven kills, playing these cards can often lead the game to a very unsatisfying conclusion. It's an inherent  weakness of the system in that victory is usually determined solely by killing enemy units, so you will often see unrealistic and tactically poor maneuvers by players trying to "game" the victory conditions. C&C games as a rule frequently reward players for ordering units to rush into the open field to pick off weakened units and score cheap kills. Again, this can make for a game in which early tactical maneuvering becomes irrelevant in a mad dash for victory banners. There are also no options for playing racially pure armies, so fantasy races are mostly relegated to mercenary work. Hopefully this improves with the game's expansions, but for now mixed armies are the rule.

Set-up time is also an issue. Considering how quickly this game plays out, it usually takes longer to prepare for battle than to fight one. While this may be the most realistic aspect of BattleLore, my group is a bit put off by a game that takes thirty minutes to prepare and only fifteen to play. Nevertheless, it is a fun game and well worth having and playing. Usually I'm up for it, just not on organized game days...

RATING: 10
Tactical fantasy hex-grid wargame that uses a unique mechanic for ordering troops, typical dice combat, and a fun hero-based spell deck for casting powers of awesomeness. Though there are times you feel at the mercy of bad dice rolls and swear that if you only had the right command card you would pull off the next Hannibal-crossing maneuver, the gameplay has a surprising amount of control. The system has just enough randomness to make you not a perfect commander with a perfect army (realism is a term sometimes thrown about for such an effect) and yet you never feel a complete slave to the Fates.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]MARVELLOUS BOARD-SET WARGAME. PERFECT EVEN.[/b]

A big box game replete with top-quality components, cards, rules, and board. Also comes with a funky set of coloured battle dice, similiar to the ones used in M'44.

The game plays quickly and easily, with surely the prettiest rulebook ever to grace this land, helping out with any sticky points in the ruleset.
BattleLore is a great deal of fun to play, with enough room for tactical maneuvring to quench the thirst of a wargamer, and light enough to appeal to any other gamer with a hankering for this type of thing.

I will not be buying C&C:Ancients now - there simply is no need: Battlelore fills the gap between miniature wargaming and boardgaming nicely.
The morale rules are a neat idea, and battleback is very nasty indeed, reflecting the formations of olden-times warfare, gifting your troops with a "free" stab back at whoever annoys them. Master the morale of your army and you will win the day, which is just as it should be. The lore "magic" system is also well implemented, allowing a player to mitigate his poor combat rolls by powering up his "lore" for some god-almighty megaspell to devastate the opposition on the battlefield.

The game is powered by cardplay, and the tactical playing of these cards will make or break your strategy during a battle. The winner is always the player who manages their pool of cards the best - make the most of what you're dealt, to coin a phrase - rather than the player who rolls lucky on their battle dice.

Big monsters spice up the stew wonderfully, and these MUST be handled with care by the opposing force, because a loose cannon like the giant spider, can reap terrible damage upon the flanks of a goblin army, twitching its mandibles menacingly before wading through the lowly rank-and-file, gutting the odd grunt who strays too close. You have been warned!

I must say that the game's design is quite brilliant, IMHO. Count me in as a fan of THE SYSTEM! Also plays well solo, if you cannot find a suitably geeky friend. Though, I very rarely play this solo, admittedly.

Some gamers ramble on about the luck factor in the game. Ignore those people. Large-scale combat is a messy business, and unpredictable too - not that I've fought in any wars, but you know what I mean (though I have handled a sword and shield in battle against hordes numerous: the delights of LARP). What I'm trying to say is this game is a blast, and the luck involved is all part of the fun. To the naysayers I declare: have some cheese to go with your whine!

[b]THE BENEVOLENT KING:[/b]

1. Brilliant rules. Brilliant.
2. Superb dice.
3. Great board.
4. Hordes of miniatures.
5. Beautiful rulebook.
6. Excellent tactic & magic cards.
7. Bolt-on expansion systems.
8. Giant monsters!
9. Cool morale system.
10. Effective battle-back rules. Nasty.
11. Perfect, board wargame.
12. Gives an epic mass battle feel to a board-set game, and for a fraction of the price.
13. Implements terrain advantages/disadvantages simply but very effectively. Advance on a hill, defended by bold troops at your peril.

[b]THE BLACK PRINCE:[/b]

1. Dicefest.
2. Bad streak of cards can be very bad.
3. Swing factor with a few Lore cards.
4. Slightly comical fantasy setting.
5. Bendy miniatures.
6. Certainly not as "pure" a game fighting system as Memoir, but still monstrously good.

I shall certainly buy some of the expansions for this scampy little puppy, though I haven't figured out yet what type of extra units I'd like to see. A guess a dragon or a gaggle of trolls could go down a storm. Though you certainly don't need any more expansions to get endless enjoyment from BL, out of the original box.

My personal preference, as of this snapshot of time, is to play BL as a medieval battle-game; keeping the goblin/dwarf/fireball malarky to a minimum. I do like all of that, and more, but have a thirst for longbowman and spears at present. You know of what I speak - I can get dwarves any day of the week in D&D. Oh, yes.

My copy also came with the FREE earth elemental and hill giant. Cashback!

I have the DRAGONS, HORRIFIC HORDES, TROLL & COUNTRY, CALL TO ARMS, CREATURES, and 100 YEARS expansions.

Its a good marriage between tactics and stupid luck, and thats what I really want in a war game.  The fantasy theme doesn't hurt either.

Still need to try it with the advanced lore rules.

RATING: 10
This is a great, light wargame.  However, there are two reasons why this is not as good as Battles of Westeros:
1) It is unrealistic to have the same chance to damage heavy and light units
2) Ordering units by 1/3 sections of the map can cause annoying games if your units get too concentrated in one section.  
On the other hand, this game is more solo friendly.

RATING: 7
An absolutely mountain of components here.  Makes set up slightly annoying when trying to find a specific army piece, but this isn't a huge deal.
I've never really been a fan of miniature games like Warhammer Fantasy Battle, etc., this is about as close as I like to get.  What it does, it does well, though the potential to get totally screwed with the order cards always looms like a shadow, and prevents an otherwise solid strategy game from realising it's potential.
